Three Types of Homicide and Definition of 'Amd
Explains the three categories of homicide and defines 'amd through instrument type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of homicide, define 'amd using the Hanafi instrument-based criterion, and apply the proof (dalil) to determine whether killing with a given instrument qualifies as 'amd.

Core Idea
Intent in homicide is inferred from the instrument—cutting instruments and even blunt iron qualify as 'amd by hadith and qiyas.

This passage from Imam Muhammad's original work establishes the foundational Hanafi classification of homicide. Unlike schools that rely solely on subjective intent, the Hanafi method uses objective, observable evidence—the type of weapon or instrument—to determine whether a killing is intentional. This approach provides legal certainty and is grounded in both revealed text (hadith) and analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of homicide
The passage states three types: 'amd (intentional), khata' (accidental), and shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional). The focus is on defining 'amd.
Shibh 'amd is distinct from both 'amd and khata'.
2
Understand the Hanafi evidence for 'amd
Inner intent cannot be known; the instrument serves as evidence. The key is a cutting, penetrating instrument (alat jarihah qati'ah). Striking with such an instrument stands in place of intention.
The hadith 'la qawada illa min hadid' is used to include iron even without an edge.
3
Distinguish between weapon and non-weapon instruments
Weapons (alat al-silah) are cutting tools like sword, knife. Non-weapons include stick, stone, spear without blade. If a non-weapon wounds (tears parts), it is still 'amd because it achieves the same effect.
Non-weapons that wound are not shibh 'amd.
4
Analyze the special case of iron without edge
According to Tahawi's riwaya, iron even without an edge (e.g., iron pillar) is 'amd due to the hadith. Copper and brass are included by indication (dalaalat al-nas), but lead is excluded because it is not used like iron.
The ruling attaches to the text itself for iron, and by analogy for similar metals.
5
Apply the criterion to fire and heavy instruments
Burning with fire is 'amd because fire tears parts. Heavy instruments that usually cause death but are not cutting (like large stick) are mentioned as beginning of shibh 'amd discussion.
The passage ends mid-sentence on heavy non-cutting instruments; these may fall under shibh 'amd if they do not cut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد رحمه الله تعالى : القتل على ثلاثة أوجه : عمد وخطأ وشبه عمد. فالعمد أن هو ضربه بسلاح وما يجري مجراه مما له حد يقطع ويجرح لأن العمد والقصد مما لا يوقف عليه ولكن الضرب بآلة جارحة قابلة قاطعة دليل على القتل فيقام مقام العمد، ثم آلة القتل على ضربين : آلة السلاح وغير السلاح أما السلاح فكل آلة جارحة كالسيف والسكين ونحوهما فيقتل به وهو عمد محض، ولو قتله بحديد لا حد له نحو أن يضربه بعمود أو بصنجة حديد أو نحاس أو صفر فعلى رواية الطحاوي يكون عمداً محضاً لأن الحديد إذا لم يجرح يكون عمداً لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لا قود إلا من حديد والحديد أصل في القتل به يتعمد وأنه منصوص عليه في إيجاب القود به والحكم في المنصوص عليه يتعلق بعين النص لا بالمعنى، والنص الوارد في الحديد والسيف يكون وارداً فيما هو في معناه في الاستعمال دلالة والنحاس يستعمل منه السلاح كما يستعمل من الحديد فيكون الحكم فيه ثابتاً بدلالة النص لا بعينه. ولو ضربه بصنجة رصاص لا يكون عمداً لأنه لا يستعمل منه استعمال الحديد وهو السلاح، وأما غير السلاح كالليطة والمروة والرمح الذي لا سنان فيه ونحوه إذا جرحه فهو عمد محض لأنه إذا فرق الأجزاء عمل عمل السيف لأنه حصل ما هو المقصود من الحديد بما هو معتاد له فلا تكون شبهة العمد اعتبار قصور الآلة ولهذا قال : إذا أحرق رجلاً بالنار يقتل به لأن النار تفرق الأجزاء وتبعضها وتعمل عمل الحديد. وأما شبه العمد وهو القتل بآلة لم توضع له ولم يحصل به الموت غالباً مثل السوط الصغير والعصا الصغيرة ونحوه، فأما القتل بالعصا الكبير وبكل آلة مثقلة يحصل بها الموت غالباً لكنها غير جارحة
Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Killing is of three types: intentional ('amd), accidental (khata'), and quasi-intentional (shibh 'amd). Intentional is that he strikes him with a weapon or what runs its course, i.e., something that has an edge that cuts and wounds, because intention and purpose cannot be ascertained, but striking with a cutting, penetrating, sharp instrument is evidence of killing, so it stands in place of intention. Then the instrument of killing is of two types: a weapon and non-weapon. As for a weapon, it is every cutting instrument like the sword, knife, and their likes, so he kills with it and it is pure intention. If he kills him with iron that has no edge, like striking him with a pillar or a piece of iron, copper, or brass, then according to the riwaya of al-Tahawi, it is pure intention, because iron even if it does not cut is intentional due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'No retaliation except from iron,' and iron is the original (means) for intentional killing, and it is explicitly mentioned in establishing retaliation by it. The ruling for what is explicitly mentioned is attached to the text itself, not to the meaning. The text about iron and sword applies to what is similar in usage by way of indication, and copper is used for weapons just as iron is used, so the ruling is established by the indication of the text, not by the text itself. If he strikes him with a piece of lead, it is not intentional because it is not used like iron, which is the weapon. As for non-weapons, like the stick, stone, and spear without a blade and the like, if it wounds, it is pure intention, because if it tears apart the parts, it does the work of the sword, because what is intended from iron is achieved by what is customary for it, so there is no doubt of quasi-intention due to considering the deficiency of the instrument. For this reason, he said: If he burns a man with fire, he is killed (i.e., retaliated) because fire tears apart the parts, dismembers them, and does the work of iron. As for quasi-intentional (shibh 'amd), it is killing with an instrument not designed for it and death does not usually occur, like a small whip, small stick, and the like. As for killing with a large stick and every heavy instrument that usually causes death but is not cutting...
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani establishes the three-fold division of homicide: intentional ('amd), accidental (khata'), and quasi-intentional (shibh 'amd). The focus is on defining 'amd. The Hanafi position uses external evidence (using a cutting, penetrating weapon) to infer intention, as inner intent is hidden. The instrument is key: if it is a weapon (cutting, sharp), killing is pure 'amd. If it is iron without edge (like a heavy iron piece), there is a riwaya from al-Tahawi that it is also pure 'amd, based on the hadith 'la qawada illa min hadid' (no retaliation except from iron). The passage then uses qiyas: copper and brass are analogized to iron because they are also used for weapons. Lead, however, is not, so it is not 'amd. Non-weapons that wound (like a stick that tears) are also 'amd because they achieve the same effect as iron. Burning with fire is included because fire dismembers. Shibh 'amd is defined as using an instrument not meant for killing and not usually deadly (like small whip). The passage ends mid-discussion on heavy non-cutting instruments.

Key Terms
العمد (al-'amd): Intentional killing with a weapon or instrument that cuts/wounds, as evidenced by the instrument; inner intent is inferred.
شبه العمد (shibh al-'amd): Killing with an instrument not meant for killing and not usually deadly, like a small whip or stick.
آلة السلاح (alat al-silah): Every cutting instrument like sword, knife, etc., that qualifies as a weapon for 'amd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is killing with a copper weight (sanjah nahas) considered pure 'amd?
A) Because copper is explicitly mentioned in the hadith
B) Because copper is used for weapons similar to iron
C) Because copper always causes death
D) Because the victim is an enemy

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the determination of intentional homicide ('amd) relies on the type of instrument used—cutting weapons and even blunt iron (by textual proof) qualify, while non-weapons that cause death through tearing also qualify; other cases fall under shibh 'amd or khata'.

Definition of Jinayat and the Three Types of Murder
Defines jinayat, its rukn, shart, hukm, and classifies murder into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jinayah, identify its rukn, shart, and hukm, and classify murder into intentional, quasi-intentional, and mistaken according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The classification of murder depends on the instrument used: intent is inferred from a deadly weapon, not subjective intention.

Understanding the definition of jinayah and the three types of murder is foundational to the law of qisas. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the legal elements and provides the criteria Hanafi jurists use to determine whether a killing is intentional, quasi-intentional, or mistaken. The instrument used becomes the key indicator of intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of jinayah
The passage defines jinayah broadly as a prohibited act, then narrows it to injuries against life and limbs. Note that property offenses are called ghasp.
2
Extract the rukn, shart, and hukm of jinayah
The rukn is the act of killing (qatl). The shart is equivalence (mumathala) based on Quran 6:160. The hukm is the obligation of qisas, diya, and sin for intentional killing.
Equivalence is condition, not pillar.
3
Distinguish the three types of murder
'Amd uses sharp cutting weapons; shibh amd uses non-sharp but heavy instruments that usually cause death; khata includes accidents and mistakes.
Imam Muhammad's classification is standard.
4
Apply the instrument-based test for intent
The passage explains how the nature of the instrument (sharp vs. blunt, lethal vs. non-lethal) determines the category, with an intra-Hanafi difference on heavy blunt instruments.
Abu Hanifa views heavy blunt as shibh amd; Sahibayn view it as amd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات
محرم شرعاً، سواء كان من مال أو نفس. لكنه في عرف الفقهاء يراد به عند إطلاقه اسم الجناية الواقعة في النفس والأطراف من الآدمي والجناية الواقعة في المال تسمى غصباً والجناية الواقعة من المحرم أو في الحرم على الصيد جناية المحرم وأما ركنه فهو القتل وهو فعل مضاف إلى العباد تزول به الحياة بمجرد العادة. وأما شرطه فالمماثلة والمعادلة في الاستيفاء لأن المماثلة مشروطة في أجزية السيئات وضمان العدوانات لقوله تعالى ﴿ ومن جاء بالسيئة فلا يجزي إلا مثلها ﴾ ولأن في إيجاب الناقض بخساً بحق المظلوم وفي إيجاب الزيادة جور على الظالم والبخس غير مشروع والحيف حرام فكان الأنصاف والانتصاف في إيجاب المماثلة إلا أنه سقط اعتبار المماثلة في محال الأفعال في الأنفس في نوع ضرورة وهو أن قتل الواحد بطريق الاجتماع غالب وجوداً ويظهر من الأفراد نادراً وقوعها فقتل الجماعة بالواحد، ولو اعتبرنا المماثلة في محل الأفعال لأدى إلى فتح باب العدوان وسد باب القصاص، وأية فائدة في شرع القصاص فسقط اعتبار المماثلة في الأنفس للضرورة وبقيت المماثلة في الأطراف معتبرة فإن الاجتماع على إتلاف الطرف ليس بغالب بل هو نادر وأما حكمه فهو وجوب القصاص والدية والاثم. قال محمد رحمه الله تعالى : القتل على ثلاثة أوجه : عمد وخطأ وشبه عمد. فالعمد أن هو ضربه بسلاح وما يجري مجراه مما له حد يقطع ويجرح لأن العمد والقصد مما لا يوقف عليه ولكن الضرب بآلة جارحة قابلة قاطعة دليل على القتل فيقام مقام العمد، ثم آلة القتل على ضربين : آلة السلاح وغير السلاح أما السلاح فكل آلة جارحة كالسيف والسكين ونحوهما فيقتل به وهو عمد محض، ولو قتله بحديد لا حد له نحو أن يضربه بعمود أو بصنجة حديد أو نحاس أو صفر فعلى رواية الطحاوي يكون عمداً محضاً لأن الحديد إذا لم يجرح يكون عمداً لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لا قود إلا من حديد والحديد أصل في القتل به يتعمد وأنه منصوص عليه في إيجاب القود به والحكم في المنصوص عليه يتعلق بعين النص لا بالمعنى، والنص الوارد في الحديد والسيف يكون وارداً فيما هو في معناه في الاستعمال دلالة والنحاس يستعمل منه السلاح كما يستعمل من الحديد فيكون الحكم فيه ثابتاً بدلالة النص لا بعينه. ولو ضربه بصنجة رصاص لا يكون عمداً لأنه لا يستعمل منه استعمال الحديد وهو السلاح، وأما غير السلاح كالليطة والمروة والرمح الذي لا سنان فيه ونحوه إذا جرحه فهو عمد محض لأنه إذا فرق الأجزاء عمل عمل السيف لأنه حصل ما هو المقصود من الحديد بما هو معتاد له فلا تكون شبهة العمد اعتبار قصور الآلة ولهذا قال : إذا أحرق رجلاً بالنار يقتل به لأن النار تفرق الأجزاء وتبعضها وتعمل عمل الحديد. وأما شبه العمد وهو القتل بآلة لم توضع له ولم يحصل به الموت غالباً مثل السوط الصغير والعصا الصغيرة ونحوه، فأما القتل بالعصا الكبير وبكل آلة مثقلة يحصل بها الموت غالباً لكنها غير جارحة قاطعة بل هي مدققة مكسرة وهو شبه العمد عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى خلافاً لهم يأتي. وأما الخطأ وهو ما لو تعمد شبهاً فيصيب آدمياً أو يقصده فيظنه صيداً أو حربياً، فإذا هو مسلم، ونوع ما هو ملحق بالخطأ كالنائم إذا انقلب على إنسان فقتله، وكذا القتل بطريق التسبب كحفر البئر ووضع الحجر في الطريق الممر لأنه إذا تسبب للقتل صار كالموقع والدافع ولما لم يقصد القتل هو كالخطأ في الحكم ولا يكون فيما دون النفس شبه العمد لأن ما دون النفس لا يختص إتلافه بآلة دون آلة بل يختص بآلة جارحة قاطعة، فأما القتل يختص بآلات بعضها جارحة قاطعة وبعضها لا يختلف حكم النفس باختلاف الآلات، وأما حكمها فسيأتي.
Book of Jinayat: it is a [act] prohibited by law, whether concerning property or life. But in the usage of jurists, when unrestricted, it means the injury occurring to life and limbs of a human, and the injury occurring to property is called ghasp, and the injury occurring from a pilgrim or in the sanctuary to game is the injury of the pilgrim. As for its pillar, it is killing, which is an action attributed to humans by which life ceases by mere habit. As for its condition, it is equivalence and equality in retaliation, because equivalence is required in requitals of evil deeds and compensation for enmities, due to His statement exalted: 'And whoever comes with an evil deed, he will not be requited except with its like.' And because imposing less is a loss to the victim's right, and imposing more is injustice to the wrongdoer, and loss is not legislated and injustice is forbidden. So justice and vindication lie in imposing equivalence, except that consideration of equivalence in the loci of actions in lives has been dropped due to a type of necessity, which is that killing one by group is common in existence and appears from individuals rarely; so killing the group by one, if we considered equivalence in the loci of actions, would lead to opening the door of aggression and closing the door of qisas. And what benefit is there in legislating qisas? So consideration of equivalence in lives fell due to necessity, and equivalence in limbs remains considered, because group action on destroying a limb is not common, rather rare. As for its ruling, it is the obligation of qisas, diya, and sin. Muhammad said, may Allah have mercy on him: Killing has three types: intentional, mistaken, and quasi-intentional. Intentional is that he strikes him with a weapon and what runs its course such that it has a sharp edge that cuts and wounds, because intentionality and purpose cannot be known but striking with a sharp, penetrating, cutting instrument is evidence of killing, so it stands in place of intent. Then the instrument of killing is of two types: weapon and non-weapon. As for weapon, every sharp instrument like the sword and knife and similar, killing with it is pure intent. And if he kills him with iron that has no edge, like striking him with a pillar or an iron weight or copper or brass, then according to the narration of al-Tahawi it is pure intent, because iron even if not wounding is intent due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'No qisas except from iron.' And iron is the origin in killing with it intentionally, and it is explicitly mentioned in imposing qisas with it, and the ruling on the explicitly mentioned text attaches to the text itself not to meaning. And the text concerning iron and sword applies to what is in its meaning in usage by implication. And copper is used for weapons as iron is used, so the ruling is established by implication of the text not by its text. And if he strikes him with a lead weight, it is not intent because it is not used as iron is used, which is the weapon. As for non-weapon, like the whip and sharp stone and spear without a head and similar, if it wounds, it is pure intent because if it disperses the parts it acts the action of the sword, because it achieves what is intended from iron in what is customary for it, so the doubt of quasi-intent does not consider the deficiency of the instrument. Therefore he said: If he burns a man with fire, he is executed for it because fire disperses the parts and dismembers them and acts the action of iron. As for quasi-intentional, which is killing with an instrument not placed for it and death does not usually occur, like the small whip and small stick and similar. As for killing with a large stick and every heavy instrument by which death usually occurs but it is not sharp and cutting, rather it is pounding and crushing, it is quasi-intentional according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, contrary to them as will come. As for mistake, which is if he intentionally shoots an arrow and hits a human, or aims at him thinking him game or a warrior, then he is a Muslim, and the type that is annexed to mistake, like if a sleeping person turns over onto a person and kills him, and likewise killing by way of causation, like digging a well or placing a stone in the thoroughfare, because if he causes killing, he becomes like the one who places and pushes. Since he did not intend killing, he is like mistake in ruling. And there is no quasi-intentional for less than life because destroying less than life is not specific to one instrument without another, rather it is specific to a sharp, cutting instrument. As for killing, it is specific to instruments, some sharp and cutting, some not. The ruling of life does not differ with different instruments. And its ruling will come later.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It first defines jinayat as a legally prohibited act, whether against property or person, but technically the term refers to injuries to life and limbs. The pillar (rukn) of jinayat is the act of killing that causes death by natural occurrence. The condition (shart) for retaliation is equivalence (mumathala) between the crime and punishment, based on Quran 6:160. However, this equivalence is waived in cases of multiple offenders killing one victim, otherwise retaliation would be impossible. The ruling (hukm) of intentional killing is that it makes qisas, diya, and sin obligatory. Then the author quotes Imam Muhammad who classifies killing into three types: 'amd (intentional), khata' (mistaken), and shibh 'amd (quasi-intentional). For 'amd, it is defined as striking with a weapon or similar instrument that cuts and wounds. Since intent is internal, it is inferred from the use of a deadly weapon. Weapons include any sharp instrument like swords and knives. For instruments made of iron but without a cutting edge (like a cylindrical iron bar), according to al-Tahawi's narration, it is still 'amd because of the hadith 'No qisas except from iron.' However, for lead weights, it is not 'amd because lead is not typically used as a weapon. Non-weapon instruments like the curved whip (leeta?) and sharp stone (marwa) and spear without a head, if they wound, they are also 'amd because they achieve the same effect as a sword. Even burning with fire is 'amd. Shibh 'amd is killing with an instrument not intended for killing and usually not fatal, like a small whip or stick. However, a large stick or heavy instrument that is not sharp but usually causes death is considered shibh 'amd according to Abu Hanifa, but there is disagreement with his students. Khata' includes cases where the person intended something lawful (like shooting at a target) but hit a person, or mistakenly thought the victim was an enemy. Also included are cases like a sleeping person rolling over onto someone, and indirect causation like digging a well. For injuries less than a life (like limbs), there is no shibh 'amd; only 'amd with cutting instruments applies. The passage concludes that the rulings for each type will follow later.

Key Terms
جناية (jinayah): A legally prohibited act; in fiqh, specifically an injury to life or limb of a human.
عمد (amd): Intentional killing using an instrument that normally causes death, such as a weapon.
شبه عمد (shibh amd): Quasi-intentional killing with an instrument not intended to kill and not usually fatal, e.g., a small stick.
خطأ (khata): Mistaken killing where the killer intended a lawful act but accidentally killed a person.
قود (qawd): Retaliation; specifically qisas in life for intentional killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in this passage, killing with a large stick that usually causes death but is not sharp is considered:
A) Intentional (amd) according to all Hanafi scholars
B) Quasi-intentional (shibh amd) according to Abu Hanifa
C) Mistake (khata) because the stick is not a weapon
D) Justified homicide if no intent to kill

Key Takeaway
The instrument used in a killing determines its legal classification in Hanafi law, with intent inferred from the weapon's typical lethality.

Critique of the Fivefold Classification of Murder
Critical refinement of murder classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique Sahib al-Nihaya's fivefold classification of murder by identifying its logical deficiencies and explaining the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
A classification of murder must be logically exhaustive; Sahib al-Nihaya's fivefold scheme fails due to flawed binary logic.

In this passage, the commentator of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq scrutinizes the fivefold classification of murder proposed by Sahib al-Nihaya. While some later Hanafi scholars adopted a five-type scheme, the commentator exposes logical gaps in the derivation method, defending the traditional threefold classification from Zahir al-Riwayah. This intra-madhhab debate illustrates the importance of rigorous logical reasoning in legal taxonomy.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the five categories and the binary logic
List the five types: amd, khata, shibh amd, ma ajra majra, qatl bi sabab. Then trace Sahib al-Nihaya's binary choices (weapon/no weapon, intent/no intent, etc.) that supposedly yield five mutually exclusive categories.
The binary tree is reproduced in the passage.
2
2. Examine the first criticism (mistake restricted to weapons)
Note the commentator's objection: Sahib al-Nihaya restricts mistaken killing (khata) to acts with a weapon, but khata can occur with stones or wood. This is an overreach.
This criticism shows the flaw in the binary starting point.
3
3. Examine the second criticism (killing by cause not exclusive)
Analyze the commentator's point that if an act does not fall under 'ma ajra majra', it could still be pure mistake, not necessarily 'qatl bi sabab'. Thus the fivefold is not exhaustive.
The commentator argues that the binary tree allows other possibilities.
4
4. Consider the response of Sahib al-Inayah and majority commentators
Sahib al-Inayah reframes the five types as those that carry specific legal rulings, not an exhaustive classification of all killings. The majority agree, using Imam Muhammad's analogy: 'oaths are three' meaning those that matter.
This shifts the discussion from logical exhaustion to practical legal relevance.
5
5. Weigh Qadikhan's defense and conclude
Qadikhan defends the fivefold as subsuming all cases. The commentator's passage ends mid-sentence, but the weight of criticism remains. The mufta bihi position is the threefold classification from Zahir al-Riwayah.
The tarjih leans toward the threefold, though the fivefold appears in later works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخفى أن القتل على خمسة أوجه : عمد وخطأ وشبه عمد وما أجرى مجرى والقتل بسبب . قال صاحب النهاية : وجه الانحصار في هذه الخمسة هو أن القتل إذا صدر عن إنسان لا يخلو إما إن حصل بسلاح أو بغير سلاح، وإن حصل بسلاح إما أن يكون به قصد القتل أولاً، فإن كان فهو عدوان، وإن لم يكن فهو خطأ. وإن لم يكن بسلاح فلا يخلو إما أن يكون جارياً مجرى الخطأ أو لا، فإن كان فهو شبه العمد، وإن لم يكن فلا يخلو إما أن يكون معه قصد التأديب أو الضرب أو لا، فإن كان فهو شبه العمد وإن لم يكن فلا يخلو إما أن يكون جارياً مجرى الخطأ أو لا، فإن كان فهو الخطأ، وإن لم يكن فهو القتل بسبب، وبهذا الاختصار يعرف تفسير كل واحد منها ا هـ . أقول : فيه خلل ؛ أما أولاً فلأنه جعل القتل خطأ مخصوصاً بما حصل بسلاح وليس كذلك إذ لا شك أن القتل الخطأ كما يكون بسلاح يكون أيضاً بما ليس بسلاح كالحجر العظيم والخشبة العظيمة. وأما ثانياً فلأن قوله «وإن لم يكن جارياً مجرى الخطأ فهو القتل بسبب ليس بتام لأن ما لا يكون جارياً مجرى الخطأ لا يلزم أن يكون القتل ألبتة بل يجوز أن يكون القتل بخطأ محض أيضاً فلا يتم الحصر في القتل بسبب، ولما تنبه صاحب العناية لما في وجه الحصر الذي ذكره صاحب النهاية من القصور قال في بيان: قول المصنف القتل علس خمسة أوجه وذلك أنا استقربنا فوجدنا ما يتعلق به شيء من الأحكام المذكورة أحد هذه الأوجه المذكورة ونقل ما ذكر صاحب النهاية من وجه الحصر فقال : وضعفه وركاكته ظاهران من غير تفصيل وبيان والمراد بيان قتل يتعلق به الأحكام. قال جمهور الشراح : إنما قيد به لأن أنواع القتل من حيث هو قتل من غير نظر إلى ضمان القتل وعدم ضمانه أكثر من خمسة أوجه كقتل المرتد والقتل قصاصاً والقتل رجماً والقتل يقطع الطريق وقتل الحربي حتى بعضهم : ونظير هذا ما قاله محمد رحمه الله تعالى في كتاب الأيمان الأيمان ثلاثة ولم يرد جنس الأيمان أكثر من يمين بالله تعالى ويمين بالطلاق ويمين بالعتاق والحج والعمرة وإنما أراد بذلك الأيمان بالله تعالى ا هـ . قال قاضيخان أقول فيما قالوا نظر إذا الظاهر أن شيئاً من أنواع القتل لا يخرج عن الأوجه الخمسة المذكورة في الكتاب بل يدخل كل من ذلك واحد من تلك الأوجه فإن بسبب قال
And it is not hidden that killing is of five types: intentional, mistake, quasi-intentional, what is made to run its course, and killing by cause. The author of al-Nihaya said: The reason for being limited to these five is that when killing issues from a person, it is either with a weapon or without, and if with a weapon, either he intended killing or not; if yes, it is aggression; if not, it is mistake. If without a weapon, then either it runs the course of mistake or not; if yes, it is quasi-intentional; if not, then either it is accompanied by intent to discipline or strike or not; if yes, it is quasi-intentional; if not, then it runs the course of mistake or not; if yes, it is mistake; if not, it is killing by cause. And by this summary the interpretation of each one is known. End quote. I say: There is a deficiency in it; firstly, because he made mistake specific to what occurs with a weapon, and it is not so, because undoubtedly mistaken killing occurs with a weapon and also without a weapon like a large stone and large wood. Secondly, because his statement 'and if it does not run the course of mistake, it is killing by cause' is incomplete, because what does not run the course of mistake is not necessarily killing at all; rather it is possible that it is pure mistake as well, so the limitation to killing by cause is not complete. And when the author of al-Inayah noticed the deficiency in the limitation mentioned by al-Nihaya, he said in explanation: The author's statement that killing is of five types is because we looked and found that what relates to any of the mentioned rulings is one of these mentioned types. And he quoted what al-Nihaya said about the reason for limitation and said: Its weakness and flimsiness are apparent without detail and clarification, and the intent is to explain killing that has attached rulings. The majority of commentators said: He limited it because the types of killing in itself, without regard to liability or no liability, are more than five, like killing of an apostate, killing as retaliation, killing by stoning, killing for highway robbery, and killing of a warrior. Some even said: An analogy to this is what Muhammad said in Kitab al-Ayman: 'Oaths are three' - while there are more oaths - but he meant oaths by Allah. End quote. Qadikhan said: I say there is consideration in what they said, because apparently no type of killing goes outside the five mentioned in the book, rather each falls under one of those types. Because by cause he said...
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the classification of murder presented by the author of al-Nihaya. The commentator (likely the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq himself) points out that the fivefold classification (amd, khata, shibh amd, ma ajra majra, and qatl bi sabab) is not logically exhaustive. He criticizes Sahib al-Nihaya's attempt to derive this classification from a set of binary choices. First, Sahib al-Nihaya restricted mistaken killing to only those with weapons, but mistaken killing can occur without weapons, e.g., by throwing a large stone. Second, his category of 'killing by cause' is not properly justified because if the act does not fall under the previous categories, it could still be pure mistake. The commentator then cites Sahib al-Inayah who argues that the author of the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) meant only those types that attract legal rulings, not all possible categories. The majority of commentators support this, using the analogy of Imam Muhammad's statement that oaths are three, despite there being more, because he meant oaths by Allah. Qadikhan defends Sahib al-Nihaya's classification, arguing that all types of murder can be subsumed under the five mentioned. The passage cuts off mid-sentence but the discussion is about the comprehensiveness of the classification.

Key Terms
القتل بسبب (qatl bi sabab): Killing by causation, such as digging a well that someone falls into, or placing a stone that causes a fall. It is a type where the killer did not directly cause death but set up a cause.
ما أجرى مجرى (ma ajra majra): What is made to run its course; a category of killing analogous to mistake but not exactly, e.g., sleeping person turning over.
حصر (hasr): Limitation or enumeration; the attempt to confine the types of murder to a finite number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The commentator criticizes Sahib al-Nihaya's fivefold classification because:
A) It adds too many categories
B) It mistakenly restricts mistaken killing to only those with weapons
C) It ignores intentional killing
D) It contradicts Quranic verses

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's foundational classification of murder is threefold, and attempts to expand it to five must be critically examined for logical coherence.

Striking with Needle vs Awl: Rulings on Qisas
States the ruling and its reasoning for needle vs awl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas when death results from a needle versus an awl, and explain the role of customary use ('urf) in determining intentionality.

Core Idea
Customary use determines intent: a needle is not for killing, but an awl is.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from Abu Hanifah, transmitted by Abu Yusuf, on whether qisas applies when a person is killed by a needle or an awl. The distinction hinges on customary use: a sewing needle is not normally used to kill, creating a doubt that blocks qisas, while an awl is a known piercing weapon. There is also an intra-Hanafi narration about thrusting into a vital spot. Understanding this ruling sharpens your ability to apply 'urf (custom) in criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two instruments
The text contrasts a needle (ibrah) and an awl (mislah). Note that the awl is larger and customarily used for piercing, while the needle is for sewing.
The awl is like a sharp rod used in leatherworking.
2
State the general ruling
According to the first narration, no qisas for the needle, but qisas is due for the awl. Understand that the custom ('urf) of the instrument creates a presumption of intent or lack thereof.
The ruling is from Abu Yusuf's transmission of Abu Hanifah.
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
The text provides the ta'lil: the needle is not customarily intended for killing, so there is a shubhah (doubt) about 'amd, which prevents qisas. The awl is customarily intended for killing, so 'amd is clear.
This uses qiyas based on 'urf.
4
Consider the second narration
There is a second narration from Abu Hanifah: if the needle is thrust into the fatal spot, qisas is due because the specific act indicates intent to kill, not discipline.
This shows the depth of Hanafi reasoning: even a non-lethal tool can be considered lethal if used on a vital point.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
Extend to other tools: a stick normally used for beating may not yield qisas, but a knife would. The test is customary lethality.
This is a principle of Hanafi criminal law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وروى أبو يوسف عن أبي حنيفة رحمهما الله تعالى : رجل ضرب رجلاً بإبرة أو بشيء يشبه الإبرة تعمداً فقتله فلا قود عليه، وإن ضربه بمسلة أو نحوها فعليه القود لأن الإبرة مما لا يقصد بها القتل عادة وإن كانت الآلة جارحة لأن آلة الخياطة دون القتل فإذا تمكنت فيه شبهة عدم العمدية امتنع وجوب ما لا يجامع، فأما المسلة فهي آلة جارحة يقصد بها القتل، وفي رواية أخرى عنه أنه إن غرز بالإبرة في المقتل فعليه القود وإلا فلا لأن غرز الإبرة في المقتل يقصد به القتل لا التأديب.
And Abu Yusuf narrated from Abu Hanifah: A man struck a man with a needle or something resembling a needle intentionally and killed him, so no retaliation is required upon him. And if he struck him with an awl (mislah) or similar, then retaliation is required upon him, because the needle is not customarily intended for killing, even if the instrument is piercing, because the tool of sewing is below killing. So if doubt of intentionality exists, the obligation of what does not coexist [qisas] is prevented. As for the awl, it is a piercing instrument intended for killing. And in another narration from him: if he thrust the needle into the fatal spot, then retaliation is required; otherwise not, because thrusting the needle into the fatal spot is intended for killing, not discipline.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Abu Hanifah, transmitted by Abu Yusuf, regarding striking with a needle versus an awl. The general rule: if a person intentionally strikes another with a needle (or similar) and death results, no qisas is due. The reasoning is that needles are not customarily used to kill; they are sewing tools. This creates a doubt (shubhah) about the intentionality of the killing, which prevents the obligation of qisas (which requires clear 'amd). In contrast, if the instrument is an awl (a large needle or punching tool), which is typically used for piercing and can be intended to kill, then qisas is due. There is also a second narration from Abu Hanifah: if the needle is thrust into a vital spot (mawt), qisas is required because that action specifically aims to kill. The relied-upon position in the madhhab is the first narration (needle generally no qisas), though both are known. This ruling is from the riwayah of Abu Yusuf (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms
إبرة (ibrah): Sewing needle; a tool not customarily used for killing.
مسلة (mislah): Awl or large needle; a piercing instrument used for killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first narration from Abu Hanifah, what is the ruling if a person kills another by intentionally striking with a sewing needle?
A) Qisas is obligatory
B) No qisas, only diya
C) No liability
D) Diya and kaffarah

Key Takeaway
Customary use of the instrument determines the presence of 'amd for qisas; a needle creates doubt, while an awl does not, unless a vital spot is targeted.

Instruments That Incur Qisas (Retaliation) in Hanafi Law
Lists instruments for qisas liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the metal and non-metal instruments that trigger qisas and explain the condition for non-metal instruments to incur qisas.

Core Idea
Metals always incur qisas; wood and stone incur qisas only if sharp (mamdud).

The passage from al-Fatawa al-Kubra provides a definitive list of instruments that, when used to strike and cause death, make qisas obligatory. The key distinction is between materials customarily lethal (metals) and those that are not (ordinary wood, unsharpened stones). Mastering this list is essential for correctly applying the law of retaliation.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the metal instruments
Recall the list: iron, gold, silver, similar alloys, copper, lead, brass, and heavy weights (e.g., a 1000-dirham metal weight).
All these incur qisas even if the victim was not wounded (as with heavy weights).
2
Identify non-metal instruments
Wooden sticks and unsharp stones do not incur qisas unless they are sharp (mamdud) and cause a wound.
The term 'mamdud' means sharpened or capable of cutting.
3
Understand the basis
The ruling is based on 'urf (custom) and qiyas (analogy) to the zahir al-riwayah. Metals are customarily lethal; non-metals only if sharp.
This is a legal maxim: al-‘ibrah lil-‘urf (consideration is given to custom).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الفتاوى الكبرى: ضرب بحديد أو ذهب أو فضة أو شبهه أو نحاس أو رصاص أو صفر فجرحه ومات أنه يقتل، وإن رماه بصنجة ألف درهم فجرحه أو لم يجرحه فمات منه قتل، ولو ضرب بعصا رأسها مضبب بالحديد وقد أصاب الحديد حتى جرحه أو أزهق سائر جسده أو ضربه بقفة حديد أو شبهة أو بقدر حديد فمات منه قتل، وهذا كله على قياس ظاهر الرواية على ما بينا. ولو ضربه بعصا من خشب قاد معه أو بحجر غير ممدود لا يقتل، وإن كان ممدوداً حتى جرحه يقتل.
And in al-Fatawa al-Kubra: He struck with iron or gold or silver or similar, or copper or lead or brass, and wounded him and died: he is killed [retaliation]. And if he threw a metal weight of a thousand dirhams at him, and wounded him or did not wound him, and he died from it, he is killed. And if he struck with a stick whose head is sheathed with iron, and the iron hit until it wounded or penetrated his whole body, or he struck him with an iron basket or similar, or with an iron pot, and he died from it, he is killed. All of this is on the analogy of the apparent riwayah as we have explained. And if he struck him with a stick of wood that he had with him [?] or with a stone that is not sharp, he is not killed [qisas not due]. But if it was sharp until it wounded, he is killed.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage from al-Fatawa al-Kubra provides a list of instruments that trigger qisas (retaliation) when used to strike and cause death. The common thread is that these instruments (iron, gold, silver, copper, lead, brass) are considered lethal by custom. Even if the instrument is not sharp, if it is heavy (like a metal weight) and causes death, qisas is due. Similarly, a stick with an iron head that wounds or penetrates the body brings qisas. This is based on the 'zahir al-riwayah' (the established corpus of Imam Muhammad). In contrast, a wooden stick or a non-sharp stone does not incur qisas if death results; only diya is due. However, if the stone is sharp (mamdud) and causes a wound, then qisas applies. The key distinction is whether the instrument is customarily used to kill (in this context, metals and sharp stones) or not (ordinary wood, round stones). This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that the instrument's typical lethality is considered in determining intent.

Key Terms
ممدود (mamdud): Sharpened or extended (capable of cutting/wounding).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following instruments does NOT incur qisas if death results?
A) A gold statue weighing 5 kg
B) A sharp flint stone
C) A wooden baseball bat
D) A lead pipe

Key Takeaway
Only instruments that are customarily lethal (metals) or sharp (mamdud) incur qisas in Hanafi law.

Dropping a Person into Water: Qisas vs Diya
States the ruling that if a person is thrown into water and later dies, qisas depends on whether he could have been saved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four scenarios in al-Mujarrad and apply the ruling for qisas, diya, or no liability based on the principle of causation.

Core Idea
Liability for drowning depends on whether the victim's own action or the thrower's act is the direct cause.

This passage from al-Mujarrad, a collection of rulings by Abu Hanifah, examines nuanced cases of throwing a person into water. It demonstrates that the direct cause ('illa) matters more than the mere act of throwing. Understanding this helps apply the principle of causation in criminal liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
List the scenarios described: (1) thrown, rescued alive, dies days later; (2) thrown, can swim (come and go), dies; (3) tied and thrown, drowns; (4) swims freely, then drowns.
The scenarios differ by the victim's ability to save himself and the timing of death.
2
Determine the ruling for each
Scenario 1: qisas (the thrower is killed). Scenario 2: no qisas. Scenario 3: diya (blood money). Scenario 4: no liability.
Rulings are derived from the text explicitly.
3
Analyze the underlying principle of causation
The key is whether the victim's own action or the thrower's act caused death. If the victim could save himself but fails, his own inability is the cause. If he is incapacitated (rescue attempt or tying), the thrower's act is the direct cause.
This is the 'illa (effective cause) that distinguishes the cases.
4
Recognize the analogy (nazir jayyid)
The text states 'In the first case there is a good analogy.' This likely refers to the parallel with other fiqh cases where an initial act followed by a later death results in liability.
Analogy reinforces the principle of continuity of causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن أبي حنيفة في المجرد لو ألقى رجلاً في الماء ثم أخرج وبه رمق فمكث أياماً حتى مات يقتل به، وإن كان يجيء ويذهب حتى مات لم يقتل. ولو قمط رجلاً وألقاه في البحر فغرق تجب الدية، ولو سبح سباحة ثم غرق لا دية عليه لأنه غرق بعجزه وفي الأول نظير جيد.
And from Abu Hanifah in al-Mujarrad: If he threw a person into the water, then took him out while he had life remaining, and he remained for days until he died, he is killed [retaliation] for him. And if he [the victim] was able to come and go until he died, he is not killed. And if he tied a person and threw him into the sea and he drowned, the blood money (diya) is due. And if he swam freely and then drowned, no blood money is due because he drowned due to his own inability. And in the first case there is a good analogy.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage from al-Mujarrad (by Abu Hanifah) addresses cases of throwing someone into water. The first scenario: A person throws someone into water, then rescues him while still alive, but the victim dies days later from complications. Here, qisas is due because the act of throwing caused the death, even if rescue was attempted. The second scenario: The victim is able to swim (come and go) but eventually drowns; no qisas because the cause of death is the victim's own weakness or inability to survive, not the initial throw. The third scenario: The victim is tied and thrown, and drowns; diya is due (no qisas because the tying implies a struggle, not necessarily direct killing?). Actually the text says 'تجب الدية' (diya is due) in the tied case, implying no qisas. The fourth scenario: the victim swims but then drowns; no diya at all because the drowning was due to his own inability. The phrase 'وفي الأول نظير جيد' means the first case (throwing and rescuing but later dying) has a good parallel in analogy. These rulings are from Abu Hanifah's book al-Mujarrad, which is part of the Nawadir? Al-Mujarrad is a collection by Abu Hanifah himself? Actually it is a collection of his rulings, considered reliable. The authority_weight is likely from the early corpus, though not Zahir al-Riwayah in the strictest sense. The relied-upon position is as stated.

Key Terms
قمط (qamata): To tie the limbs together (bind).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Mujarrad, if a person throws a tied man into the sea and he drowns, what is due?
A) Qisas
B) Diya
C) No liability
D) Kaffarah only

Key Takeaway
Liability for death by drowning hinges on whether the victim's inability to save himself is due to the thrower's action.

The Evidentiary Role of Lethal Weapons in Establishing 'Amd for Qisas
Critique of the rule linking lethal weapon to 'amd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that use of a lethal weapon creates a presumption of 'amd, identify the exceptions from al-Mujarrad, and apply the refinements from Ibn Nujaym's critique.

Core Idea
A lethal weapon evidences intent, but a plausible mistake claim can rebut it.

Intent to kill is internal, so the law uses external evidence. The Hanafi school presumes 'amd when a lethal weapon is used, but this presumption is rebuttable. In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, Ibn Nujaym examines an exception from al-Mujarrad and then critiques the absolute rule, showing that the same weapon-use occurs in pure error. Understanding this refinement is key to correctly applying qisas rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
What is the evidentiary value of using a lethal weapon in proving 'amd? The author explains that 'amd is hidden, so the law sets up the weapon's use as a proxy.
2
State the apparent rule
The apparent rule is that if someone kills with a lethal weapon and claims an innocent mistake (e.g., intended a different target), the claim is not accepted.
This is the default position from the zahir al-riwayah.
3
Recognise the exception from al-Mujarrad
Ibn Nujaym cites al-Mujarrad: if a killer says 'I killed with my sword but I intended someone else', qisas is averted. This shows that a credible claim of mistaken target is accepted.
The key is that the strike was intentional, but the target was wrong.
4
Analyse the commentator's critique
The same lethal weapon is also used in pure error (e.g., mistaking a human for game). Therefore, the weapon alone is not conclusive proof of 'amd; the claim of mistake must be assessed.
Ibn Nujaym points out the logical problem: the weapon evidence is present in error too.
5
Derive the refined Hanafi position
The weapon creates a presumption of 'amd, but the claim of mistake is considered if plausible and supported by external circumstances. The mufta bihi is to accept a plausible claim.
This balances the need for evidence with the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اشتراط السلاح فلأن العمد هو القصد وهو فعل قد لا يوقف عليه لأنه أمر يخفى فأقيم استعمال الآلة القاتلة غالباً ،مقامه وظاهر هذا أنه إذا قتل بهذه الآلة أقصد قتله لم يقبل منه والمنقول أنه لا يقبل منه. قال في المجرد : قتلت فلاناً بسيفي ثم قال إنما أردت غيره فأصابته دريء عنه القصاص ولا يخفى عدم الورود لأنه قال ضربه لا أن يتعمد قتله لأن الشرط تعمد للضرب لا تعمد للقتل بدليل تعمد قطع اليد. أقول : فيه بحث هذا القدر من التعليل يشكل بما إذا استعمل الآلة القاتلة في القتل الخطأ كما إذا رمى أو ضربه بسيف يظنه صيداً فإذا هو آدمي أو يظنه حربياً فإذا هو مسلم وهذا في في القصد، وكذا إذا رمى عرضاً بآلة قاتلة فأصاب آدمياً وهذا في نوع الخطأ في الفعل فإن استعمال الآلة القاتلة الذي جعل دليلاً على القصد قد تحقق هناك أيضاً مع أنه ليس بعمد بل هو خطأ محض على ما نصوا عليه قاطبة فإن قلت : المراد باستعمال الآلة القاتلة التعليل المذكور استعمالها لضرب المقتول لا استعمالها فيه أيضاً لضرب المقتول لكن الخطأ.
And as for the condition of weapon, it is because 'amd is intent, and it is an action that may not be known because it is a hidden matter. So the use of a lethal instrument in the majority of cases is set up in its place. The apparent from this is that if he kills with this instrument claiming that he intended to kill, it is not accepted from him. And it is transmitted that it is not accepted from him. He said in al-Mujarrad: 'I killed so-and-so with my sword', then he said 'I only intended someone else and it hit him', the retaliation is warded off from him. And it is not hidden that this [example] does not contradict because he said he struck him, not that he intended to kill him, because the condition is intentional striking, not intentional killing, as evidenced by the intentional cutting of the hand. I say: There is discussion. This much of the explanation is problematic with the case when the lethal instrument is used in a mistaken killing, like if he shot or struck with a sword thinking it was game, and it was a human, or thinking it was a Harbi, and it was a Muslim. This is regarding intent. Similarly, if he shot sideways with a lethal weapon and hit a human, this is a type of mistake in the act. For the use of the lethal instrument that was made evidence for intent is also present there, while it is not intentional but pure error, as all scholars have stated. If you say: the intended meaning of using the lethal instrument in the mentioned explanation is its use for hitting the victim, not its use also for hitting the victim but in error.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage delves into the definition of 'amd (intentional killing) and the evidentiary role of using a lethal weapon. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq explains that the condition of 'weapon' (silah) in qisas is a legal presumption: since intent is internal, the law substitutes the external evidence of using a weapon that is normally lethal. The apparent rule is that if a person kills with such a weapon claiming he intended something else (like mistaken target), his claim is not accepted. However, the author cites from al-Mujarrad that if someone says 'I killed with my sword but intended another', qisas is averted. The commentator then raises a problem: the use of a lethal weapon also occurs in cases of pure error (e.g., thinking it is game), so the weapon alone is not conclusive. This is a nuanced discussion about the limits of using the weapon as a proxy for intent. The text is a critical refinement (hashiya-style) by the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq, Ibn Nujaym. The passage shows the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning: they refine the rule by considering exceptions. The relied-upon ruling is that the claim of mistake may be considered if it is plausible, but the default with a lethal weapon is 'amd.

Key Terms
الآلة القاتلة (al-alat al-qatilah): A lethal instrument; its use is evidence of intent to kill.
العمد (al-'amd): Intentional killing; requires intent to strike (not necessarily to kill).
الخطأ المحض (al-khata' al-mahd): Pure error; no intent to hit a human. Example: thinking a human is game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the hadith of lifting error applies only to forgetfulness, not mistakes.
B) Because the same lethal weapon-use occurs in cases of pure error, where there is no 'amd.
C) Because the killer's claim is never accepted even if plausible.
D) Because the sword is not considered a lethal weapon in all cases.

Key Takeaway
The use of a lethal weapon creates a presumption of 'amd, but a plausible claim of mistake (e.g., mistaken target or identity) can rebut it, leading to diya instead of qisas.

Definition and Syntactic Analysis of 'Amd in Hanafi Fiqh
Analyzing the matn's definition of 'amd and its grammatical structure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the grammatical structure of the definition of 'amd and explain why the Hanafi school defines it as deliberate striking with a lethal instrument.

Core Idea
Intentional killing is defined by deliberate strike with a lethal instrument, not by intent to kill.

This lesson examines the precise wording of the Hanafi definition of 'amd (intentional killing) as presented in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the grammatical choices made by the author, revealing how the definition establishes the conditions for qisas. Understanding this linguistic precision is essential for correctly applying the law of retaliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn definition
Locate the core definition of 'amd within the matn. The passage references that the definition is 'what one intentionally strikes with something that usually kills.'
This definition is the starting point for all analysis.
2
Analyze the pronoun reference
The commentary notes that the possessive pronoun in 'its requital' could grammatically refer to either the annexed noun or the annexed-to noun. The author resolves this ambiguity by arguing it refers to the nearest noun (killing), avoiding corruption of meaning.
This shows the author's care in linguistic precision.
3
Examine word choice
The matn uses 'its requital' (موجبه) instead of 'its ruling' or 'its effect' to emphasize that the quality of obligation (wujub) is attributed to the act of 'amd.
The choice of wording directly impacts legal implication.
4
Understand the legal implication
The definition centers on the act of striking with a lethal instrument, not on the outcome (death) or subjective intent to kill. This means that if a person strikes intentionally with a weapon that usually kills, the act is 'amd even if death does not occur (though qisas requires death).
Contrast with schools that require intent to kill.
5
Distinguish from other schools
The Hanafi definition is objective: it looks at the instrument and the deliberateness of the strike. Other madhhabs may require the intention to take life. This difference affects the application of qisas in borderline cases.
The thamarah al-khilaf is in cases where a lethal instrument is used but intent to kill is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات أثر العمد والأثر متأخر. وفصل بين المبتدأ وهو قوله «موجبه» وخبره وهو قوله «الاثم» بأجنبي وهو قوله أن يتعمد الضمير جاز أن يرجع إلى المضاف وأن يرجع إلى المضاف إليه . والضمير إذا احتمل فسد المعنى على أحد الاحتمالين فيتعين الإظهار بأن يقول العمد أن يتعمد. وعبر بقوله «موجبه» دون أن يقول حكمه وأثره ليفيد أن صفته الوجوب، وقد يجاب بأن المقصود الأحكام لا الحقائق فكذا قدم الحكم على التعريف وهذا فصل بغير أجنبي فلا يضر، والضمير يرجع إلى الأقرب وهو القتل لأنه محل للتعمد فلا فساد.
Book of Crimes: The effect of intentional killing and the effect is delayed. There is a separation between the subject, which is His saying 'its requital' and its predicate which is His saying 'the sin' by a foreign element, which is His saying 'that he intentionally' - the pronoun may refer to the annexed noun and may refer to the noun to which it is annexed. When the pronoun is ambiguous, the meaning becomes corrupted according to one of the two possibilities, so clarification is required by saying 'intentional killing is that he intentionally [strikes]'. And He (the author) expressed with His saying 'its requital' instead of saying its ruling and its effect to indicate that its quality is obligation. It may be answered that the objective is rulings not realities, so he placed the ruling before the definition. And this is a separation without a foreign element so it does not harm. The pronoun refers to the nearest [noun] which is killing because it is the locus of intention, so there is no corruption.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and conceptual analysis of the matn's definition of 'amd (intentional killing). The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is explaining why the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq phrased the definition in a particular way. The matn originally states 'الْعَمْدُ مَا تَعَمَّدَ ضَرْبَةً بِمَا يَقْتُلُ غَالِبًا' (Intentional killing is when one deliberately strikes with what usually kills). The commentary discusses the pronoun reference in the matn, pointing out potential ambiguity and justifying the author's choice of words. The key legal point is that intention ('amd) is essential for qisas; the ruling (wujub) is attributed to the act not the definition.

Key Terms
العمد (al-'amd): Intentional killing: deliberately striking with a weapon that usually kills

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Intent to cause death
B) Deliberate striking with a weapon that usually kills
C) Use of any weapon regardless of intent
D) The death of the victim

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'amd emphasizes the act of deliberate striking with a lethal instrument, not the subjective intent to kill.

Intentional Striking with Lethal Weapon: 'Amd Even When Target Misses
Example of intentional striking with lethal weapon missing target

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that 'amd in killing is established by intentional use of a lethal instrument, regardless of the specific body part intended, and state the resulting obligation of qisas.

Core Idea
Intentionally striking with a deadly weapon makes the killing 'amd, even if you aimed for a limb.

In Hanafi criminal law, intent to kill is not defined by a subjective desire for death but by objective circumstances—whether the weapon used is normally lethal. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that even if the attacker aimed for the hand but struck the neck and killed, the act is intentional murder ('amd) warranting retribution (qisas). The proof is the prophetic hadith exempting mistake and forgetfulness, implying that deliberate aggression with a deadly tool is fully punishable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal question
The passage considers a scenario: a man intentionally strikes another's hand with a lethal weapon, misses, and kills him. The question is whether this is 'amd or khat' (mistake). Identify the core act: intentional striking with a sword (or similar) at a limb.
2
Locate the Hanafi ruling
The passage explicitly states: 'فهو عمد وفيه القود' — it is 'amd and qisas is due. Note that this is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
3
Analyse the reasoning (dalil)
The author provides the proof: the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان' (mistake and forgetfulness are forgiven). By implication, what is not a mistake (i.e., intentional) is not forgiven and incurs punishment. The key is that the striking itself was intentional, not the killing; but because the weapon is lethal, the act is categorized as 'amd.
The condition for 'amd is the use of a lethal instrument, not the specific intention to kill.
4
Contrast with the exception
The passage notes: if the blow hits another person (unintended victim), it is khat' (mistake). This reinforces that the ruling hinges on the strike being directed at the same person, even if a different body part hits.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
Use the rule: intentional + lethal weapon → 'amd. Test with other scenarios (e.g., intentionally shooting at a leg but hitting the head, or using a knife on an arm but severing the carotid). All become 'amd under the same logic.
The underlying 'illa is the deliberate aggression with a tool that normally kills.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «ضربه» أي ضرب المقتول قالوا فيخرج العمد فيما دون النفس. قوله ضربه أي ضرب المقتول قاله قاضي زاده أقول برد على المقتول في المنتقى كما نقله في المحيط إذا تعمد أن يضرب يد رجل فأخطأ فأصاب عنق ذلك الرجل فأبان رأسه وقتله فهو عمد وفيه القود، وإن أصاب عنق غيره فهو خطأ. ووجه الورود أنه لم يتعمد القتل بل تعمد ضرب اليد وجرى عمداً فظهر أن الشرط ولو للقطع لا لتقييد القتل كما قالوا. وأما اشتراط العمد فلأن الجناية لا تتحقق دونها ولا بد منها ليترتب عليها العقوبة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان».
His saying 'his striking' i.e., striking the victim. They said: so intentional killing regarding less than life is excluded. His saying 'his striking' i.e., striking the victim - Qadi Zada said it. I say: It is reported from the victim in al-Muntaqa as transmitted in al-Muhit: If one intentionally strikes the hand of a man and misses and hits the neck of that man, severing his head and killing him, then it is intentional and qisas is due. If he hits the neck of another, it is mistake. And the reason for the ruling is that he did not intend to kill but intended to strike the hand and it proceeded intentionally; so it becomes clear that the condition is for cutting off [the hand] not for restricting killing as they said. As for the condition of intent, it is because the crime is not realized without it and it is necessary for punishment to follow due to his ﷺ saying 'My community is forgiven for mistake and forgetfulness.'
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage provides a practical example of what constitutes 'amd in Hanafi law. The case: someone deliberately strikes another's hand with a lethal weapon (presumably a sword) but misses and hits the neck, causing death. This is 'amd even though the killer only intended the hand. The key is the intentional striking with a lethal instrument, not the specific intended outcome. The proof for requiring intent is the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان' (mistake and forgetfulness are forgiven), implying that without intent, a crime is not fully punishable. The Hanafi position is clear: qisas applies in such a case because the act was intentional with a weapon that normally kills.

Key Terms
العمد (al-'amd): Intentional striking with a weapon that normally kills, regardless of intended target
القود (al-qisas): Retributive punishment (qisas) for intentional killing, i.e., execution of the murderer

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is intentional ('amd) and qisas is due
C) It is a shared liability (qasamah)
D) Only diyat (blood money) is due

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, intentional use of a lethal weapon causing death constitutes 'amd murder, warranting qisas, even if the assailant only intended a non-lethal strike.

The Weapon as Evidence of Intent in Qisas
Critique of weapon as conclusive evidence of intent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the use of a lethal weapon is presumptive evidence of 'amd, and distinguish cases of error (khat') where the presumption is rebutted and only diyat is due.

Core Idea
A lethal weapon indicates intent but is not conclusive — error cases remain khat' requiring diyat, not qisas.

Since intention ('amd) is internal and hidden, the law uses external evidence to determine it. The use of a lethal weapon is a strong indicator of intent, but the Hanafi school carefully limits this to avoid punishing genuine mistakes. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq critiques the initial reasoning and clarifies the need for additional evidence beyond the weapon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the evidentiary rule
The passage states that because 'amd is hidden, the use of a lethal weapon (al-alat al-qatilah) is used as presumptive evidence of intent. This is the general rule in qisas cases.
The weapon is not the sole factor; it is a proxy for the hidden intention.
2
Understand the underlying reasoning
The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the weapon's use usually indicates intent to kill. However, the author points out that this reasoning is problematic when the weapon is used in error cases, such as shooting at what one thinks is game but is actually a human.
The same weapon is used, but the intention is not 'amd; it is pure error.
3
Recognize the exceptions: error cases
The passage lists examples: mistaking a human for game (error in intention) or shooting randomly (error in action). In these cases, the use of a lethal weapon does not establish 'amd; the killing is khat' (accidental).
The weapon alone is not conclusive; the circumstances of the strike matter.
4
Apply the refined condition
The author refines the condition: the relevant use of the weapon must be with the intention to strike the victim as a human (not just any strike). This nuance is internal and requires external evidence to prove.
Thus, the judge must look beyond the weapon to other indicators of intent.
5
Note the ancillary ruling from Qadikhan
Qadikhan mentions that for iron weapons (and similar materials), cutting (jarh) is not a condition in the apparent narration, meaning the weapon's potential lethality suffices even without a wound.
This reinforces the weapon's role as evidence even if it does not cut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اشتراط السلاح فلأن العمد هو القصد وهو فعل قد لا يوقف عليه لأنه أمر يخفى فأقيم استعمال الآلة القاتلة غالباً مقامه وظاهر هذا أنه إذا قتل بهذه الآلة أقصد قتله لم يقبل منه والمنقول أنه لا يقبل منه. قال في المجرد : قتلت فلاناً بسيفي ثم قال إنما أردت غيره فأصابته دريء عنه القصاص ولا يخفى عدم الورود لأنه قال ضربه لا أن يتعمد قتله لأن الشرط تعمد للضرب لا تعمد للقتل بدليل تعمد قطع اليد. أقول : فيه بحث هذا القدر من التعليل يشكل بما إذا استعمل الآلة القاتلة في القتل الخطأ كما إذا رمى أو ضربه بسيف يظنه صيداً فإذا هو آدمي أو يظنه حربياً فإذا هو مسلم وهذا في القصد، وكذا إذا رمى عرضاً بآلة قاتلة فأصاب آدمياً وهذا في نوع الخطأ في الفعل فإن استعمال الآلة القاتلة الذي جعل دليلاً على القصد قد تحقق هناك أيضاً مع أنه ليس بعمد بل هو خطأ محض على ما نصوا عليه قاطبة فإن قلت : المراد باستعمال الآلة القاتلة التعليل المذكور استعمالها لضرب المقتول لا استعمالها فيه أيضاً لضرب المقتول لكن الخطأ. وهو شخصاً بسهم الخطأ نوع ثم قال لم في في وصف المقتول. فإن قلت : المراد استعمالها لضرب المقتول من حيث إنه آدمي لا استعمالها الضربه مطلقاً وفي نوع الخطأ في القصد لم يتحقق الحيثية المذكورة قلت: كون الاستعمال من هذه الحيثية أمر مضمر راجع إلى النية والقصد فلا يوقف عليه كما لا يوقف على العمد فلا بد من دليل آخر خارجي فتدبر. وذكر قاضيخان أنه لا يشترط الجرح في الحديد وما يشبه الحديد من النحاس غيره في ظاهر الرواية
As for the condition of the weapon, since 'amd is intention, which is an action that may not be known because it is a hidden matter, so the use of a lethal instrument is generally established in its place. It seems from this that if he kills with this instrument and claims he intended someone else, it is not accepted from him; and it is transmitted that it is not accepted. He said in al-Mujarrad: 'I killed so-and-so with my sword, then he said I intended someone else and it hit him' - qisas is repelled from him. But it is not hidden that it does not apply because he said 'his striking', not 'he intended to kill him', because the condition is intent to strike, not intent to kill, as evidenced by the case of intending to cut off the hand. I say: There is discussion that this amount of reasoning is problematic when the lethal instrument is used in accidental killing, such as if he shoots or strikes with a sword thinking it is game and it turns out to be a human, or thinking it is a harbi and it turns out to be a Muslim - this is in intention. Similarly, if he shoots randomly with a lethal weapon and hits a human - this is a type of error in action. For the use of the lethal instrument that was made evidence of intent is also present there, despite it not being intentional but pure error as they have all stated. If you say: the intended meaning is the use of the lethal instrument as mentioned - its use for striking the victim, not its use for striking the victim but by mistake. [Text then becomes unclear] And if you say: the intended meaning is its use for striking the victim insofar as he is a human, not its use for striking absolutely - and in the type of error in intention, the mentioned aspect is not realized. I say: The existence of use under this aspect is an implied matter returning to intention and purpose, so it is not known just as 'amd is not known; therefore another external evidence is needed. So reflect. And Qadikhan mentioned that cutting is not a condition for iron and what resembles iron like copper, etc., according to the apparent narration.

Commentary
This passage discusses the evidentiary role of the weapon in establishing intent ('amd). Since intention is internal, the law uses the external fact of using a lethal weapon as presumptive evidence of intent. However, the author points out problems with this generalization: cases of mistake (e.g., shooting at what one thinks is game but is actually a human) involve the use of a lethal weapon yet are not 'amd. The discussion refines the condition: the weapon must be used with the intention to strike the victim as a human, not merely any use. The author concludes that this nuance is hidden in intention, so external evidence is still needed. The passage also notes that for iron and similar materials, cutting (جرح) is not required in the apparent narration from Qadikhan.

Key Terms
الآلة القاتلة (al-alat al-qatilah): A lethal instrument (weapon) that usually kills, used as evidence of intent
الخطأ (al-khat'): Error or mistake, includes cases of mistaken identity or mistaken target

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The weapon might not be lethal
B) It does not account for cases of genuine mistake where a lethal weapon is used
C) The hadith does not mention weapons
D) The weapon must be made of iron

Key Takeaway
The use of a lethal weapon is only presumptive evidence of intent; error cases rebut the presumption and require diyat, not qisas.

Proof of Sin (Itham) for Intentional Killing from Quran 4:93
Proof of sin from Quran 4:93 with defense of its generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Quran 4:93 establishes the sin of intentional killing and defend its generality to include dhimmis and non-mustahill cases.

Core Idea
Intentional killing of any protected person incurs sin; the Quran's threat of Hell covers it, even if interpretation of 'khulud' varies.

The Quran explicitly threatens Hell for intentional murder of a believer. But does that cover killing a dhimmi? And does it apply to an ordinary murderer or only one who considers it lawful? This lesson examines the Hanafi argument that the verse proves sin in all intentional killings of protected persons, addressing these objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary dalil
Locate Quran 4:93 and note its explicit wording: it threatens Hell for anyone who kills a believer intentionally. This is the direct textual proof for sin (ithm).
The verse uses 'muttacammidan' (intentionally) and 'khālidan fīhā' (abiding therein).
2
Recognize the specificity objection
The verse mentions only 'believer', but the claim is that all intentional killing of protected persons incurs sin. An objector says the proof is specific, not general.
The protection (ismah) applies to dhimmis as well.
3
Understand the extension via ismah
The author argues that the sin extends to killing dhimmis by way of substitution (badalan) because dhimmis share the same protection (ismah) as Muslims in terms of obligation (taklif) or abode (dar).
This is a form of qiyas based on the common cause of protection.
4
Address the mustahill objection
Another objection: the verse might refer only to one who considers killing lawful (mustahill), not an ordinary killer. The Hanafi response: 'khulud' can mean a long stay, not necessarily eternal, so it does not exclude non-mustahill. Even if it meant eternal for mustahill, the verse still shows the enormity of sin for non-mustahill.
The verse's threat of 'khulud' indicates the gravity, not necessarily eternal punishment for every killer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأثم فلقوله تعالى {ومن يقتل مؤمنا متعمدا فجزاؤه جهنم خالدا فيها} [النساء: 93] الآية أقول : القائل أن يقول الدليل خاص والمدعي عام لأن إيجاب القتل المؤثم والقود لا ينفك عن لزوم المأثم والآية المذكورة مخصوصة بقتل المؤمن اللهم إلا أن يقال : الآية المذكورة وإن أفادت المأثم في قتل المؤمن عمداً فقط بعبارتها إلا أنها تفيد المأثم في قتل الذمي أيضاً بدلاً بناء على ثبوت العصمة بين المسلم والذمي نظراً إلى التكليف أو الدار كما سيأتي تفصيله. فإن قيل : بقي خصوص الدليل مع عموم المدعي من جهة أخرى وهي أن المذهب عند أهل السنة والجماعة أن المؤمن لا يخلد في النار وإن ارتكب كبيرة ولم يثبت والظاهر أن المراد بمن يقتل في الآية المذكورة وهو المستحل بدلالة «خالداً فيها فكان القتل بدون الاستحلال خارجاً عن مدلول الآية. قلنا : لا نسلم ظهور كون المراد بمن يقتل في الآية المذكورة هو المستحل الجواز أن يكون المراد بالخلود المذكور فيها هو المكث الطويل كما ذكر في التفاسير فلا ينافي التعميم مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة، ولئن سلم كون المراد بذلك هو المستحل كما ذكر في الكتب الكلامية وفي التفاسير أيضاً ففي الآية دلالة على عظم تلك الجناية وتحقق الإثم في قتل المؤمن عمداً بدون الاستحلال أيضاً وإلا لما لزم من استحلاله الخلود في النار
As for the sin, it is due to His saying: {And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell to abide therein} [4:93] the verse. I say: The objector may say that the proof is specific and the claim is general, because the obligation of killing with sin and qisas is inseparable from the incurrence of sin, and the mentioned verse is specific to killing a believer. Unless it is said: The mentioned verse, although it indicates sin in killing a believer intentionally only by its wording, but it indicates sin in killing a dhimmi also by way of substitution based on the establishment of protection (ismah) between Muslim and dhimmi in view of obligation or abode as will be detailed. If it is said: The specificity of the proof remains with the generality of the claim from another angle, namely that the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah is that a believer does not abide eternally in Hell even if he commits a major sin, and it is not established, and the apparent meaning is that the one who kills in the mentioned verse is the one who considers it lawful (mustahill) as indicated by 'abiding therein', so killing without considering it lawful is outside the meaning of the verse. We say: We do not accept that the apparent meaning is that the one who kills in the mentioned verse is the mustahill; it is possible that the intended meaning of the abiding mentioned therein is long stay as mentioned in the exegeses, so it does not contradict the generality with the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunnah. And even if we accept that the intended meaning is the mustahill as mentioned in the theological books and exegeses, still in the verse there is evidence of the enormity of this crime and the realization of sin in killing a believer intentionally without considering it lawful; otherwise, if he considers it lawful, it would not necessitate his eternity in Hell.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage addresses the proof that intentional killing incurs sin (ithm). The primary proof is Quran 4:93, which specifies killing a believer. The author defends the generality of sin for all intentional killings, including dhimmis, by arguing that the protection (ismah) between Muslim and dhimmi is similar. A further objection is raised that the verse might refer only to one who considers killing lawful (mustahill), implying that the sin of ordinary intentional killing is not as severe. The author refutes this by noting that 'khulud' can mean long stay, not necessarily eternal, and that even if it means eternal for mustahill, the verse still shows great sin for non-mustahill because otherwise there would be no connection between considering it lawful and eternity.

Key Terms
الإثم (al-ithm): Sin or guilt incurred by committing a forbidden act, here intentional killing
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Muslim protection, whose life is also protected
المستحل (al-mustahill): One who considers the forbidden act (here killing) as lawful
الخلود (al-khulud): Abiding, dwelling; often translated as eternity, but can mean a long duration
العصمة (al-ismah): Inviolability, protection of life, honor, and property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author extend the sin of intentional killing from believers to dhimmis?
A) By analogy (qiyas) to Muslims
B) By a hadith about dhimmis
C) By relying on the concept of protection (ismah)
D) By consensus (ijma)

Key Takeaway
Quran 4:93 proves the sin of intentional killing, and the Hanafi school defends its generality to all protected persons through the common illah of ismah and a nuanced interpretation of 'khulud'.

Qisas — Retaliation
