The Sword and Qisas: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling that qisas must be done with a sword, with Shafi'i dispute and Hanafi response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the method of executing qisas and explain why the Hanafi school rejects the Shafi'i view of matching the killing method.

Core Idea
Qisas is executed only with a sword; the killing method does not dictate the execution method.

How should a murderer be executed? The Hanafi school insists on the sword, regardless of the method of killing. The Shafi'i school argues for similarity. This lesson unpacks the proof from hadith and the linguistic reasoning that anchors the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify clearly what the Hanafi school rules: qisas must be carried out with a sword (or weapon, according to some). Note that this is the muftā bihi position.
The hadith states 'No qisas except with a sword'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that execution should be done with the same means as the killing, if the killing was by a legitimate act; if by an illegitimate act (like sodomy), a similar device is used.
Shafi'i relies on the principle of equivalence.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine al-Shafi'i's reasoning: he argues by analogy—just as the crime was committed by a specific method, the retaliation should match it.
He also cites the Qur'anic principle of 'life for life' but extends it to method.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is based on the explicit hadith. They argue that the word 'qawad' in the hadith means the execution act, not the obligation, so it restricts the method. They also reject the metaphorical interpretation that would limit the hadith to obligation.
The response uses the principle that a clear text (nass) cannot be overridden by qiyas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, all qisas executions are by sword; under Shafi'i law, the method varies (e.g., burning if the killer burned, etc.).
This affects how the state carries out capital punishment for murder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما يقتص بالسيف وقال الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى : يقتص بمثل ما قتل إن قتله بفعل مشروع وإن قتله بغير فعل مشروع كلواطة يتخذ له خشبة ويفعل به كما فعل ولنا ما رواه سفيان من قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لا قود إلا بالسيف» وهو نص على نفي استيفاء القود بغير السيف فكيف يلحق به دلالة ما كان سلاحاً من غير السيف؟ وهل يتصور أنه يدل كلام واحد على نفي شي وإثباته معاً؟ والحق أن يكون المراد بالسيف في الحديث المزبور السلاح مطلقاً بطريق الكناية كما أشار إليه المصنف بقوله «والمراد به السلاح». وصرح به صاحب الكافي والكفاية حيث قالا : ولنا قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لا قود إلا بالسيف والمراد بالسيف السلاح هكذا فهمت الصحابة رضي الله تعالى عنهم. وقال في النهاية : فإن قيل يحتمل أن يكون المراد من الحديث لا قود يجب إلا بالسيف لا أن يكون معناه لا قود يستوفي إلا بالسيف قلنا القود اسم لفعل هو جزاء القتل دون ما يجب شرعاً وإن حمل عليه كان مجازاً، ولأن القود قد يجب بغير السيف كالقتل بالنار والإبرة فلم يمكن حمله عليه لوجود وجوب القود بدون القتل بالسيف وإنما السيف مخصوص بالاستيفاء اهـ.
And qisas is only with a sword. Al-Shafi'i said: qisas is with the same means used to kill if the killing was with a legitimate act; if with an illegitimate act like sodomy, then a wooden device is made and he is done to as he did. Our proof: what Sufyan narrated from the hadith 'No qisas except with a sword' — it is a clear text negating execution of qisas with other than the sword. How can analogy attach it to what is a weapon other than the sword? Can a single statement simultaneously negate and affirm? The correct view is that by 'sword' in the hadith is meant 'weapon' metaphorically, as the author indicated by saying 'the intended meaning is the weapon'. The authors of al-Kafi and al-Kifayah explicitly stated: our proof is the hadith 'No qisas except with a sword', and the intended meaning is 'weapon'; this is how the Companions understood it. In al-Nihayah: if it is said that the hadith might mean 'no qisas becomes obligatory except with a sword' not 'no qisas is executed except with a sword', we reply: 'qisas' is the name of the act that is the penalty for killing, not what becomes obligatory; if it were interpreted that way it would be metaphorical. Also qisas can become obligatory without killing by sword (e.g., killing by fire or needle), so it cannot be interpreted that way because obligation occurs without sword execution; rather the sword is specific to execution.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
The text discusses a dispute with al-Shafi'i over the method of executing qisas. The Hanafi position is that the killer is executed only with a sword, regardless of the method of killing. Al-Shafi'i says the execution should be analogous to the killing method: if the killer used a legitimate method (like beheading), the execution is similar; if an illegitimate method (like burning or sodomy), the state should devise a similar method. The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'La qawada illa bi al-sayf' (No qisas except with the sword). The commentator discusses the interpretation: some understand 'sword' to mean 'weapon', but the author leans to the literal sword. The text also refutes a possible interpretation that the hadith means qisas is wajib only if the killing was by sword, arguing that qisas can be wajib even without sword-killing (e.g., by fire), so the hadith is about execution method, not obligation.

Key Terms
قود (qawad): The act of executing qisas, i.e., retaliation in kind for murder.
سيف (sayf): Sword; in the hadith, interpreted by some Hanafis as any weapon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how must qisas be carried out?
A) In the same manner as the killing.
B) With a sword only.
C) With any weapon.
D) By throwing the killer from a height.

Key Takeaway
Qisas must be executed with a sword; the method of killing does not alter the wajib method of execution.

Qisas — Retaliation

Qisas for a Mukatab Who Kills: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ruling on qisas for a mukatab killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for a mukatab in two scenarios and explain the Sahibayn's rationale regarding ambiguity of the master's entitlement.

Core Idea
A mukatab's status at death determines qisas: assets left? ambiguity drops qisas; no assets? qisas stands.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) who commits intentional murder. The key question is whether the ambiguity in the master's basis for inheritance (guardianship if the mukatab dies free, ownership if he dies a slave) should prevent qisas. We will analyze two scenarios and the arguments of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First scenario: mukatab leaves assets (wafa) and has no heir other than his master. Second scenario: he does not leave assets and has an heir other than the master.
The ruling differs based on the presence of assets.
2
State the ruling of the Sahibayn for each
For the first scenario, qisas is not obligatory according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the relied-upon view). For the second, qisas is obligatory for the master because the kitaba dissolves and he dies a slave.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the first scenario
The Sahibayn argue that the master is definitely the heir; the ambiguity in the cause (guardianship vs. ownership) does not harm because the cause is not desired for itself but for its ruling, which is attained.
Compare with the earlier view attributed to Muhammad that ambiguity drops qisas entirely.
4
Contrast with the second scenario
When the mukatab dies without assets, his slave status is certain, so qisas applies to the master as owner.
This is distinguished from a partial emancipatee who dies without assets, where qisas does not apply due to doubt.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Both positions in the first scenario agree that qisas does not apply, but the reasoning affects how the master's claim is perceived in analogous cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكاتب قتل عمداً وترك وفاء ووارثه سيده فقط أو لم يترك وفاء وله وارث يقتص أما الأول وهو ما إذا ترك وفاء ولا وارث له سوى المال فالمذكور هنا هو قولهما، وعن محمد رحمه الله تعالى لا يجب القصاص لأن سبب الاستحقاق قد اختلف ولأن المولى يستحقه بالولاية بأن مات حراً أو بالملك إن مات عبداً فاشتبه الحال فلا يستحق لأن اختلاف السبب كاختلاف المستحق فيسقط أصلاً كما إذا كان له وارث غير المولى فصار كما لو قال لغيره يعني هذه الجارية بكذا وقال المولى زوجتها منك لا يحل له وطؤها لاختلاف الحكم. ولهما أن المولى هو المستحق للقصاص على التقديرين بيقين وهو معلوم فلا يضر مجرد اختلاف السبب لأن السبب لا يراد لذاته وإنما يراد لحكمه وقد حصل بخلاف المستشهد به لاختلاف حكم السببين ولا يدري بأيهما يحكم فلا يثبت الحل بدون تعيين السبب . وأما الثاني وهو ما إذا لم يترك وفاء له وراث غير المولى فلأنه مات رقيقاً لانفساخ الكتابة بموته لا عن وفاء فظهر أنه قتل عبداً عمداً فيكون القصاص للمولى بخلاف معتق البعض إذا قتل ولم يترك وفاء له حيث لا يجب القصاص لأن العتق في البعض لا يفسخ بموته عاجزاً، ولأن الاختلاف في أنه يعتق كله أو بعضه ظاهر فأشبه المستحق فأورث ذلك شبهة كالمكاتب إذا قتل عن وفاء.
A mukatab who kills intentionally and leaves assets, and his heir is only his master; or he does not leave assets and has an heir who is not his master - qisas is applied. As for the first scenario: if he leaves assets and has no heir other than the master, what is mentioned here is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). From Muhammad, qisas is not obligatory because the basis of entitlement differs, and because the master claims it by guardianship if he dies free, or by ownership if he dies a slave, so the situation is ambiguous and he does not deserve it, because difference in cause is like difference in claimant, so it drops entirely, like if there is an heir other than the master. It becomes like if he said to someone: 'this slave girl is for such price' and the master said 'I married her to you' - it is not halal for him to have intercourse due to difference in ruling. Their (the two's) argument: the master is definitely entitled to qisas in both cases; he is known, so mere difference in cause does not harm, because the cause is not desired for itself but for its ruling, which is attained, unlike the analogy cited because there the rulings of the two causes differ and it is unknown which to judge by, so the permissibility is not established without specifying the cause. As for the second scenario: if he does not leave assets and has an heir other than the master, then because he died a slave, the kitaba being dissolved by death without assets, it becomes clear that he was killed as a slave intentionally, so qisas belongs to the master, contrary to a partial emancipatee who kills and leaves no assets, where qisas is not obligatory because emancipation in part is not dissolved by his death insolvent, and because the difference regarding whether he is fully free or partially is apparent, so it resembles a claimant and creates doubt, like the mukatab who kills with assets.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage deals with a mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission) who commits premeditated murder. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn) from the two companions. The author presents two scenarios: (1) The mukatab leaves assets (وفاء) and has no heir other than his master. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, qisas is not obligatory because the master has a conflicting basis of entitlement: if the mukatab dies free (because the kitaba is complete), the master inherits by guardianship; if he dies a slave (if the kitaba is not complete), the master inherits by ownership. The ambiguity causes qisas to drop. Muhammad himself also says it drops. The companions argue that the master is certainly the heir regardless, so the ambiguity does not matter. The author seems to side with the two. (2) The mukatab does not leave assets and has an heir other than the master (i.e., he has a free heir). In this case, since he dies without assets, the kitaba dissolves, and he dies a slave. Thus, qisas is for the master (as owner). This is contrasted with a partially freed slave (معتق البعض) who dies without assets: qisas is not obligatory because the partial freedom creates doubt. The underlying principle is that the certainty of the legal status affects the application of qisas.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission with his master, agreeing to pay a sum in installments in exchange for freedom.
وفاء (wafa): Assets sufficient to fulfill the kitaba (manumission contract).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why does qisas not apply when a mukatab leaves assets and has only the master as heir?
A) Because the mukatab died a slave.
B) Because the master is definitely the heir and ambiguity in the cause does not harm.
C) Because the victim was not a Muslim.
D) Because the master does not have a right to qisas.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a mukatab kills and leaves assets, ambiguity in the master's basis of inheritance prevents qisas; if he dies without assets, his slave status is certain and qisas applies to the master.

Qisas — Retaliation

No Qisas When Mukatab Leaves Estate and Heir
Consensus ruling on qisas for a killed mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas when a mukatab is killed and leaves an estate and an heir, and explain the reason for the Companions' consensus.

Core Idea
When a mukatab is killed, qisas is dropped if the estate is sufficient and there is an heir, due to disagreement over whether he died free or slave.

The mukatab has a unique legal status—part slave, part free. When killed, his status at death determines who holds the right of qisas. The Companions differed on this point, leading to a consensus that qisas is not applied in case of uncertainty. This lesson unpacks that ruling and the subtle critique by Ibn Qadi Zada on the wording in al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scenario
The matn states: 'If a mukatab leaves an estate and an heir, no qisas.' Identify the condition (estate + heir) and the ruling (no retaliation).
The estate must be sufficient to fulfill the kitaba contract.
2
Trace the proof: Companions' ikhtilaf and consensus
The passage cites three Companions: Ali and Ibn Mas'ud held the mukatab died free (qisas for heir); Zaid ibn Thabit held he died a slave (qisas for master). The disagreement creates uncertainty, so qisas is dropped by consensus.
Ijma (consensus) is the primary proof here.
3
Analyze Ibn Qadi Zada's critique
Ibn Qadi Zada argues that the author of al-Hidayah should have specified 'free heir' in this scenario because if the heir is a slave, the confusion vanishes (slaves cannot execute qisas). Understand his reasoning.
This is a methodological critique, not a disagreement on the ruling.
4
Understand the defense of the absolute term
The commentator responds that 'heir' here means 'potential heir'—a slave is capable of inheriting if freed. Thus qualification is not needed; the absolute term is correct.
This distinction between actual and potential heir is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ترك وفاء ووارثا لا أي لا يقتص وهذا بالإجماع وإن اجتمع المولى والوراث لاشتباه من له الحق لأنه إن مات حراً كما قال علي وابن مسعود رضي الله عنهما فالقصاص للوارث، وإن مات عبداً كما قال زيد بن ثابت رضي الله عنه فالقصاص للمولى. قال ابن قاضي زاده على عبارة الهداية أقول : أطلق الوارث ههنا ولم يفيده بالحر وقيده في الصورة الآتية حيث قال : وإن لم يترك وفاء وله ورثة أحرار وكان الأولى أن يعكس الأمر فإنه إذا كان الوارث ههنا رقيقاً فالظاهر أنه يجب القصاص للمولى عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لكون حق الاستيفاء للمولى خاصة إذ لا ولاية للأرقاء على استيفاء القصاص فلم يشتبه من له الحق ههنا ، وأما إذا كانت الورثة أرقاء في الصورة السابقة فيجب القصاص للمولى وحده في قولهم جميعاً كما إذا كانت ورثته أحراراً لأنه مات عبداً في تلك الصورة والتقييد بالأحرار يشعر بكون الحكم في الأرقاء خلاف ذلك على أن مفهوم المخالفة معتبر عندنا أيضاً في الروايات كما صرحوا به . فإن قلت : الرقيق لا يكون وارثاً لأن الرق أحد الأمور الاربعة التي تمنع عن الأرث كما تقرر في علم الفرائض فلا إحتياج إلى تقييد الوارث بالحر بل لا وجه له لإشعاره بكون الرقيق أيضاً وراثاً قلت : المراد بالوارث هنا من كان من شأنه أن يرث والرقيق كذلك لأنه يرث عند زوال الرق لا من يرث بالفعل فيحتمل التقييد بالحرية وإلا يلزم أن لا يتم تقييد الورثة بالأحرار في الصورة الآتية أيضاً مع أنها قيدت بها في الكتاب بل في أصل الجامع الصغير للإمام الرباني .
And if he left an estate and an heir, no, meaning no retaliation, and this is by consensus, even if the master and the heirs come together, due to confusion over who has the right. Because if he died free, as Ali and Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with them) said, then retaliation is for the heir; and if he died a slave, as Zaid ibn Thabit (may Allah be pleased with him) said, then retaliation is for the master. Ibn Qadi Zada said on the statement of al-Hidayah: I say: He used the word 'heir' here in an absolute sense and did not qualify it as free, but he qualified it in the coming scenario where he said: 'And if he did not leave an estate and he has free heirs.' It would have been more appropriate to reverse the matter. Because if here the heir is a slave, then apparently retaliation is obligatory for the master according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, since the right of execution belongs solely to the master, as slaves have no authority to execute retaliation, so here it is not confused who has the right. But if the heirs are slaves in the previous scenario, then retaliation is obligatory for the master alone in all their opinions, just as if his heirs were free, because in that scenario he died a slave. The qualification with 'free' suggests that the ruling for slaves is otherwise, but the concept of contrary implication is also considered by us in the transmissions, as they stated. If you say: A slave cannot be an heir because slavery is one of the four impediments to inheritance as established in the science of faraid, so there is no need to qualify the heir as free; indeed there is no basis for it because it suggests that a slave can also be an heir. I say: By 'heir' here is meant one who is capable of inheriting, and a slave is also such because he inherits when slavery is removed, not someone who actually inherits. Therefore, qualification by freedom is possible; otherwise, the qualification of heirs as free in the coming scenario would also not be complete, whereas it is qualified as such in the book, indeed in the original al-Jami' al-Saghir of Imam al-Rabbani.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof commentary on the matn statement: 'If a mukatab leaves an estate and a heir, no retaliation.' The author explains that this is by consensus because there is confusion: scholars differ on whether the mukatab dies free (Ali and Ibn Mas'ud) or a slave (Zaid ibn Thabit). If free, the heir takes qisas; if slave, the master takes. Due to uncertainty, qisas is dropped. Ibn Qadi Zada then critiques the wording in al-Hidayah: the author should have specified 'free heir' here, because if the heir is a slave, the confusion is removed (slaves cannot execute qisas), so retaliation would be for the master. He also discusses the concept of 'heir' meaning potential heir, not actual, to justify the qualification.

Key Terms
وارث (wārith): A person who is capable of inheriting from the deceased; here it can include a slave who could inherit if freed.
إجماع (ijmā‘): Consensus of the scholars (here, of the Companions) on the rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is qisas not obligatory when a mukatab is killed and leaves an estate and a free heir?
A) Because the heir is entitled and waived the right.
B) Because the master is entitled and the heir has no right.
C) Because there is uncertainty over whether the mukatab died free or a slave, leading to confusion over who holds the right of qisas.
D) Because the mukatab's contract of kitaba was invalid.

Key Takeaway
When a mukatab is killed and leaves sufficient estate and an heir, qisas is not applied due to uncertainty over his status at death, based on the Companions' differing views.

Qisas for a Pawned Slave: Joint Consent Required
Ruling on qisas when a pawned slave is killed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas when a pawned slave is killed and explain why both pawner and pledgee must consent.

Core Idea
Qisas for a pawned slave requires joint consent of pawner and pledgee because the pawner cannot unilaterally destroy the pledgee's security right.

When a pawned slave is killed, the question arises whether the killer can be subjected to qisas. The pawner owns the slave, but the pledgee holds it as collateral for a debt. The Hanafi school rules that qisas is only valid if both parties agree. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this condition and an intra-school dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the intentional killing of a pawned slave. The ruling: qisas is not carried out until both pawner (rahin) and pledgee (murtahin) consent.
Ruling is from the matn: 'لا يقتص حتى يجتمع الراهن والمرتهن'.
2
Understand the logic: pawner's duty to protect pledgee's right
The pawner cannot unilaterally execute qisas because it would destroy the pawn (slave) without compensation, harming the pledgee's security interest in the debt. The pawner may not perform an action that nullifies another's right.
The proof is rational (dalil aqli).
3
Examine the opposing view
Some Hanafi scholars (Fakhr al-Islam in al-Uyun and al-Jami' al-Saghir) argue that qisas is never established even with consent, analogizing to a mukatab slave who leaves an estate and an heir.
The analogy: uncertainty over entitlement.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author rejects the analogy: the pledgee has no independent right to qisas (no ownership or wafa'), unlike the heir of the mukatab. Thus the case is distinct.
Key distinction: pledgee's right is only in the debt, not in retaliation.
5
Note the practical consequence
Under the main view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), qisas is allowed if both consent. Under the opposing view, only diya is paid. The mufta bihi is the main view.
The source text is cut off but clearly supports Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is not for the pawner to execute an action that leads to the nullification of another's right. It is mentioned in al-Uyun and al-Jami' al-Saghir of Fakhr al-Islam that qisas is not established for them even if they both agree, making it like the mukatab who left an estate and an heir. But the difference between them is apparent: the pledgee is not entitled to qisas because he has neither ownership nor 'wafa', so he does not resemble the one who has the right, unlike the mukatab as we explained. And in al-Uyun: A pawned slave if killed intentionally, then if both agree on qisas, they may execute retaliation according to Abu Hanifa and Abu...

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof commentary on the matn: 'If a pawned slave is killed, retaliation is not carried out until the pawner and the pledgee are together.' The author explains that the pawner cannot unilaterally execute qisas because it would destroy the pledgee's security interest (the slave is collateral for a debt). If the killer is executed, the slave is lost without replacement, harming the pledgee. Therefore, both must agree. A divergent view from al-Uyun and al-Jami' al-Saghir holds that qisas is never established even if both agree, analogizing to the mukatab case. The author rejects this analogy, arguing that the pledgee has no right to qisas (no ownership or inheritance right), so the situation is different. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with Abu Hanifa's and Abu Yusuf's position that qisas is allowed if both agree.

Key Terms
عبد الرهن (abd al-rahn): A slave who has been pawned as collateral for a debt.
راهن (rāhin): The pawner, the debtor who gives the slave as collateral.
مرتهن (murtahin): The pledgee, the creditor who holds the pawn as security for a debt.

Qisas — Retaliation
advanced
8 min
Qisas When a Slave Leaves Both Master and Free Heir
Inter-madhhab dispute with expansion and refinement by a later commentator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas when a killed slave leaves a master and an heir, and explain why the ruling is suspended in cases of uncertainty and clarified when the heir is a slave.

Core Idea
Uncertainty over the slave's status at death suspends qisas; if the heir is a slave, the master alone has the right.

When a slave is killed and leaves behind both a master and an heir, a crucial question arises: who has the right to demand retaliation (qisas)? The answer depends on whether the slave died free or a slave, an ambiguity that leads to a consensus-based suspension of qisas. This lesson unpacks the disagreement among the Companions and the Hanafi refinement that resolves the confusion when the heir is a slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue and consensus ruling
The passage states that when a killed slave leaves both a master and an heir, qisas is not carried out by consensus. The reason is that it is uncertain whether the slave died free (in which case the heir has the right) or as a slave (in which case the master has the right).
The consensus prevents hasty execution of qisas when the rightful claimant is unclear.
2
Understand the disagreement among the Companions
Ali and Ibn Mas'ud held that the slave died free, so qisas belongs to the heir. Zayd ibn Thabit held that the slave died a slave, so qisas belongs to the master. Their disagreement illustrates the uncertainty.
The Hanafi school uses this ikhtilaf to justify the suspension.
3
Follow the refinement by Ibn Qadi Zada
Ibn Qadi Zada notes that in the matn of al-Hidayah, the word 'heir' is used without qualification. He argues that if the heir is a slave, there is no uncertainty because slaves have no authority to execute qisas. Thus, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the master alone has the right.
This refinement shows that the suspension applies only when the heir is free; a slave heir does not create ambiguity.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, if a killed slave leaves a free heir, no qisas is executed. If the heir is a slave, the master may execute qisas alone. This distinction arises from the Hanafi position that slaves cannot exercise the right of retaliation.
The thamarah is that the ruling changes based on the heir's status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى يجتمع الراهن والمرتهن ولأبي المعتوه القود والصلح لا العفو بقتل وليه والقاضي وفاء، فأما إذا كان له وارث غير المولى يرشد إليه ذكر مخالف هذه المسألة في حيز قوله «وإن لم يترك وفاء وله ورثة أحرار إلى آخره فيحنئذ يصح تتميم ما حمله المصنف في تعليله بقوله لأن العتق في البعض لا ينفسخ بالعجز بأن يقال فالمولى يستحق القصاص. في البعض المملوك بالملك والوارث يستحقه في البعض المعتق بالإرث فيكون السببان راجعين إلى الشخصين فيبالي باختلافهما للإفضاء إلى المنازعة تأمل تقف واشتراط الوارث وقع اتفاقاً فإنه إذا لم يكن له وراث أيضاً الحكم كذلك لموته رقيقاً، وذكر ذلك لينبه على أنه لا فرق بين أن يكون له وارث أو لم يكن بخلاف المسألة الأولى.
قال رحمه الله : (وإن ترك وفاء ووارثا لا أي لا يقتص وهذا بالإجماع وإن اجتمع المولى والوراث لاشتباه من له الحق لأنه إن مات حراً كما قال علي وابن مسعود رضي الله عنهما فالقصاص للوارث، وإن مات عبداً كما قال زيد بن ثابت رضي الله عنه فالقصاص للمولى. قال ابن قاضي زاده على عبارة الهداية أقول : أطلق الوارث ههنا ولم يفيده بالحر وقيده في الصورة الآتية حيث قال : وإن لم يترك وفاء وله ورثة أحرار وكان الأولى أن يعكس الأمر فإنه إذا كان الوارث ههنا رقيقاً فالظاهر أنه يجب القصاص للمولى عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لكون حق الاستيفاء للمولى خاصة إذ لا ولاية للأرقاء على استيفاء القصاص فلم يشتبه من أ له الحق ههنا ، وأما إذا كانت الورثة أرقاء في الصورة السابقة فيجب القصاص للمولى وحده في قولهم جميعاً كما إذا كانت ورثته أحراراً لأنه مات عبداً في تلك الصورة والتقييد بالأحرار يشعر بكون الحكم في الأرقاء خلاف ذلك على أن مفهوم المخالفة معتبر عندنا أيضاً في الروايات كما صرحوا به . فإن قلت : الرقيق لا يكون وارثاً لأن الرق أحد الأمور الاربعة التي تمنع عن الأرث كما تقرر في علم الفرائض فلا إحتياج إلى تقييد الوارث بالحر بل لا وجه له لإشعاره بكون الرقيق أيضاً وراثاً قلت : المراد بالوارث هنا من كان من شأنه أن يرث والرقيق كذلك لأنه يرث عند زوال الرق لا من يرث بالفعل فيحتمل التقييد بالحرية وإلا يلزم أن لا يتم تقييد الورثة بالأحرار في الصورة الآتية أيضاً مع أنها قيدت بها في الكتاب بل في أصل الجامع الصغير للإمام الرباني .
Until the pawner and pawnee come together, and for the one of unsound mind there is retaliation and reconciliation, not pardon by killing his guardian, and the judge fulfills. As for if he has an heir other than the master, he is guided to it; it mentions the contrary of this issue in the context of his statement: 'If he does not leave sufficient property and he has free heirs...' to the end. At that point, the completion of what the author carried in his reasoning by his statement: 'Because emancipation in part is not nullified by inability' is valid by saying: The master deserves retaliation in the part owned by ownership, and the heir deserves it in the part inherited by inheritance; so the two causes refer to two persons, so it matters if they differ because it leads to dispute. Contemplate, you will find. And the condition of the heir occurred by agreement; for if he has no heir, the ruling is also like that because he dies as a slave. He mentioned that to indicate that there is no difference between having an heir or not, unlike the first issue.
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (If he leaves sufficient property and an heir, no, i.e., he is not retaliated against, and this is by consensus, even if the master and the heirs are together) because of confusion over who has the right. For if he died free, as Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with them, said, then retaliation is for the heir; and if he died a slave, as Zayd ibn Thabit, may Allah be pleased with him, said, then retaliation is for the master. Ibn Qadi Zada said on the wording of al-Hidayah: I say: He used the term 'heir' here without specifying 'free,' and he restricted it in the later scenario where he said: 'If he does not leave sufficient property and he has free heirs.' It would have been better to reverse the matter, for if the heir here is a slave, then apparently retaliation is due to the master according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf because the right of execution belongs exclusively to the master, since slaves have no authority to execute retaliation; so there is no confusion about who has the right here. However, if the heirs are slaves in the previous scenario, then retaliation is due to the master alone in the opinion of all of them, just as if his heirs were free, because he died a slave in that scenario. And the restriction to 'free' suggests that the ruling for slaves is contrary to that, even though the concept of opposing implication is valid with us also in the narrations, as they have clarified. If you say: A slave cannot be an heir, because slavery is one of the four things that prevent inheritance, as established in the science of inheritance, so there is no need to restrict the heir to free, and there is no justification for it because it suggests that a slave is also an heir. I say: What is meant by 'heir' here is one who is capable of inheriting, and a slave is also like that because he inherits upon removal of slavery, not one who actually inherits. Thus, the restriction to freedom is possible; otherwise, the restriction of the heirs to 'free' in the later scenario would not be established, though it was restricted to them in the book, and indeed in the original al-Jami' al-Saghir of the imam al-rabbani.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute (though also intra-Hanafi refinement) regarding the ruling on qisas (retaliation) when a slave is killed and leaves behind both a master and an heir who is free. The main ruling is that qisas is not carried out at all, by consensus, due to uncertainty over whether the deceased died as a free man (in which case retaliation belongs to the heir) or as a slave (in which case it belongs to the master). The views of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud (free) and Zayd ibn Thabit (slave) are cited. The commentator then turns to a refinement by Ibn Qadi Zada, who notes that in the matn of al-Hidayah, the word 'heir' is used without qualification, but later it is restricted to 'free heirs.' He argues that if the heirs are slaves, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, retaliation goes to the master alone because slaves lack authority to execute qisas. He also clarifies that a slave is not an actual heir but can be considered a potential heir, hence the qualification is meaningful.

Key Terms
وارث (warith): In this passage, 'heir' refers to someone who is capable of inheriting, not necessarily one who actually inherits at the moment (since a slave cannot inherit).
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment in kind for intentional homicide or injury, specifically the taking of life for life in the context of murder.
مولى (mawla): Master of a slave; the person who owns the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is qisas not executed when a killed slave leaves behind a master and a free heir?
A) Because the master and heir must agree, and they often disagree.
B) Because it is uncertain whether the slave died free or a slave, so the right is confused.
C) Because the heir is not entitled to qisas if there is a master.
D) Because slaves cannot be subject to qisas at all.

Key Takeaway
When a killed slave leaves a master and a free heir, qisas is suspended due to uncertainty; but if the heir is a slave, the master alone may execute qisas because a slave cannot exercise retaliation.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate

Qisas for a Mortgaged Slave: Consent of Pawner and Pawnee Required
Ruling with proof and intra-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for a pawned slave, explain the rationale based on the pawnee's right, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with its practical consequence.

Core Idea
A pawned slave's qisas requires consent of both pawner and pawnee; the pawner alone cannot void the creditor's security.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific case: when a slave that has been pledged as collateral (rahn) is killed. The murdered slave belongs to the pawner but is held by the pawnee as security. The key question: who can demand qisas? The Hanafi ruling is that qisas cannot be carried out unless both the pawner (owner) and the pawnee (creditor) agree. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning, the intra-madhhab disagreement, and the practical outcomes.

Guided Path
1
State the Main Ruling
Identify the act (killing a pawned slave) and the ruling: qisas is not executed until both pawner and pawnee agree. This is the foundational point.
The matn says: 'لا يقتص حتى يجتمع الراهن والمرتهن'.
2
Analyze the Rationale (Ta'lil)
The pawner alone cannot execute qisas because it would destroy the pawnee's right to the pawn as security. Qisas would cause the pawn to perish without compensation, nullifying the debt guarantee. The pawner has no right to perform an act that harms another's rights.
This is a qiyas-based argument: the illa is 'prevention of harm to the pawnee's right'.
3
Examine the Contrasting View
Al-Uyun and al-Jami' al-Saghir present an alternative: qisas is never established even if both agree, analogizing to a mukatib (slave with manumission contract) who leaves sufficient property. But the author distinguishes: the pawnee has neither ownership nor a claim of sufficiency, unlike the mukatib's heirs.
This comparison is rejected by the preferred view.
4
Map the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow qisas if both agree, with the pawner as executor. Muhammad and Zufar say no qisas, only value. Note the different positions and their reasoning.
This is a valid ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Determine the Operational Ruling
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: qisas may be executed with agreement. Other narrations (Hisham, Ibn al-Walid) detail variants regarding taking value as replacement pawn. The practical consequence: if qisas, the debt is dropped; if value, the pawnee recurs against the pawner.
Al-Marghinani prefers Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن قتل عبد الرهن لا يقتص حتى يجتمع الراهن والمرتهن) لأن الراهن لا يليه لما فيه من إبطال حق المرتهن في الدين لأنه لو قتل القاتل لبطل حق المرتهن في الدين لهلاك الرهن بلا بدل وليس للراهن أن يستوفي تصرفاً يؤدي إلى بطلان حق الغير. وذكر في العيون والجامع الصغير لفخر الإسلام أنه لا يثبت لهما القصاص وإن اجتمعا فجعلاه كالمكاتب الذي ترك وفاء وارثاً ولكن الفرق بينهما ظاهر فإن المرتهن لا يستحق القصاص لأنه لا ملك له ولا وفاء فلا يشبه من له الحق بخلاف المكاتب على ما بينا. وفي العيون : العبد المرهون إذا قتل عمداً فإن اجتمعا على القصاص فلهما أن يقتصا في قوله أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ويكون المستوفي هو الراهن. وقال محمد :وزفر لا قصاص وعلى القاتل القيمة. وفي الينابيع روی هشام عن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف أنه يؤخذ من القاتل قيمته ويكون رهناً مكانه . وروى ابن الوليد عن أبي يوسف عن أبي حنيفة أنهما إذا اتفقا على القصاص وقيمته أقل من الدين أو مثله فلهما ذلك، وإن اختلفا فلهما قيمته وتكون رهناً مكانه. ثم على قول أبي يوسف إذا اجتمعا على القصاص سقط الدين عن المرتهن في الرواية الظاهرة، وإن اجتمعا على أخذ القيمة يرجع المرتهن على الراهن بدينه كالعبد الموصى بخدمته.
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (If a slave that is a pawn is killed, qisas is not executed until the pawner and the pawnee come together) because the pawner cannot execute it due to what it entails of nullifying the pawnee's right to the debt; for if he killed the killer, the pawnee's right to the debt would be nullified because the pawn perishes without compensation, and the pawner is not entitled to execute an act that leads to the nullification of another's right. And it is mentioned in al-Uyun and al-Jami' al-Saghir of Fakhr al-Islam that qisas is not established for them even if they come together, and they made it like the mukatib who leaves sufficient property and an heir; but the difference between them is apparent, for the pawnee is not entitled to qisas because he has neither ownership nor sufficient property, so he is not like the one who has the right, unlike the mukatib as we explained. And in al-Uyun: If the pawned slave is killed intentionally, and they both agree upon qisas, they may execute qisas according to the saying of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and the executor is the pawner. Muhammad and Zufar said: No qisas, and the killer owes the value. And in al-Yanabi': Hisham narrated from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that the killer's value is taken and it becomes a pawn in its place. And Ibn al-Walid narrated from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa that if they agree on qisas and its value is less than the debt or equal, they may do so; if they disagree, they take its value and it becomes a pawn in its place. Then, according to the saying of Abu Yusuf, if they agree on qisas, the debt is dropped from the pawnee in the apparent narration; if they agree on taking the value, the pawnee recourse against the pawner for his debt, like the slave whose services are bequeathed.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding qisas for a mortgaged slave who is killed. The main ruling: qisas is not carried out until both the pawner (owner of the slave who pledged it) and the pawnee (creditor) agree. The reason is that the pawner alone cannot execute qisas because that would invalidate the pawnee's security interest. The alternative view from al-Uyun and al-Jami' al-Saghir is that qisas is never established even with agreement, comparing it to a mukatib (a slave contracting freedom). But the author argues a difference: the pawnee has no right to qisas because he has neither ownership nor a claim of sufficiency. Then, various narrations within the Hanafi school are presented: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow qisas if both agree, with the pawner performing the execution. Muhammad and Zufar say no qisas, only value. Other narrations specify that value is taken and placed as a pawn, or that if they differ, value is taken. Finally, Abu Yusuf's view on the consequences: if they agree on qisas, the debt is waived; if they take value, the pawnee recurs against the pawner.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pawn or collateral; a slave given as security for a debt.
راهن (rahin): The pawner; the debtor who pledges the slave as collateral.
مرتهن (murtahin): The pawnee; the creditor who holds the pawn as security.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has made a contract of manumission with his master to pay for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what happens if a mortgaged slave is killed and both the pawner and pawnee agree to qisas?
A) They cannot execute qisas; only the value is taken.
B) They may execute qisas, and the pawner performs the execution.
C) They may execute qisas, and the pawnee performs the execution.
D) Qisas is automatically executed without any condition.

Key Takeaway
Qisas for a pawned slave requires the consent of both the pawner and the pawnee; without both, only compensation is due.

Qisas for Slave with Multiple Rights
Proposing a more comprehensive rule for qisas on a slave with multiple right-holders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the proposed Hanafi rule for qisas on a slave with two complete rights and apply it to scenarios involving bequest, sale, and other rights.

Core Idea
For a slave with two complete rights, qisas requires the agreement of all right-holders.

The commentator suggests a more comprehensive phrasing for the original text, covering all cases where a slave is subject to multiple rights (e.g., ownership and services, or dower and khul'). This principle avoids injustice and ensures that all claimants are satisfied. We will examine the proposed rule and its applications from Hanafi fatwa works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed rule
What is the more comprehensive formulation suggested by the author? Look for the key phrase: 'If a slave with two complete rights is killed, qisas is not executed until both come together.'
The author prefers this over the original text because it covers more cases.
2
Understand the rationale
Why is this rule more appropriate and concise? It integrates cases like a slave whose person and services are bequeathed separately, a mortgaged slave, etc., all under one expression.
The terms 'two complete rights' and 'until both come together' are carefully chosen.
3
Examine key applications
Work through the examples: bequeathed slave before acceptance, slave with separate person and service rights, slave given as dower, substitute for khul', sold slave before delivery.
Each application modifies the default qisas rule based on the nature of the rights.
4
Note intra-Hanafi differences
In some cases, such as a sold slave killed before delivery, the imams differ on who has the right to qisas. Understand the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
The dispute revolves around ownership after sale and payment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال المؤلف وإن قتل عبد فيه حقان تمامان لا يقتص حتى يجتمعا لكان أولى وأخصر؛ أما كونه أولى فلأنه يشمل العبد الموصى برقبته لإنسان وبخدمته لآخر وغيره. وقولنا حقان» ليفيد أنه إذا كانا مالكين فلا بد من اجتماعهما. وكونه أخصر أظهر . وقولنا «تامان» ليخرج العبد المبيع المقتول قبل القبض كما سيأتي. وفي فتاوى الفضلي : الموصى به إذا قتل قبل أن يقبل الموصى له الوصية فلا قصاص للوارث ولا للموصى له إن اتفقا أنه مات قبل قبول الموصى له ثم بعد ذلك ينظر؛ إن قبل الموصى له الوصية رجع على القاتل بقيمته ولا ترجع الورثة بذلك والموصى برقبته لرجل وبخدمته لآخر إذا قتل عمداً فلا قصاص فيه إلا أن يجتمعا. وفي الكبرى : إن اتفقا بطل حق صاحب الخدمة ويستوفيه صاحب الرقبة وإن لم يرض صاحب الخدمة فإنه تجب القيمة على القاتل ويشتري بها عبداً آخر ويكون حاله مثل حال الأول. وفي القدوري قال أبو يوسف : العبد الممهور إذا قتل قبل قبض المرأة وبدل الخلع إذا قتل قبل قبض الزوج وبدل الصلح عن دم العمد إذا قتل في يد الغاصب عمداً، فإن شاء المالك اقتص من القاتل، وإن شاء ضمن الغاصب قيمة عبده ثم يرجع الغاصب على القاتل. وإن قتل العبد المبيع قبل القبض فالقصاص للمشتري إن أجاز البيع لأنه المالك، وإن نقص فللبائع لأن البيع ارتفع وظهر أنه المالك، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وفي العيون وفي فتاوى الفضلي : العبد المبيع إذا قتل قبل القبض عمداً يخير المشتري بين المضي والرد، فإن اختار المضي فله أن يقتص ولكن لا يكون له الاستيفاء إلا بعد نقد الثمن فقد جوزوا إجازة البيع بعد الموت هنا، ولو رد المشتري المبيع للبائع أن يقتص في قوله أبي حنيفة، وإذا أدى الثمن قال أبو. يوسف : لا يقتص البائع. وعند محمد تجب القيمة في الوجهين لاشتباه المستحق. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد : رجل قطع يد عبد رجل أو شجه رجل ثم إن المولى باعه ثم رد عليه بعيب بقضاء قاض أو وهبه المولى من إنسان ثم رجع في الهبة بقضاء أو بغيره ثم مات العبد من الجناية فإن مولى العبد يرجع على الجاني بجميع قيمته. وفي نوادر بشر عن أبي يوسف : لو أن أمة قطعت يدها خطأ وباعها المولى من إنسان على أنه بالخيار وردت على المولى فماتت عنده من القطع فعلى القاطع قيمتها ،تامة وإن كان القطع عمداً رأت القصاص استحساناً. وفي نوادر داود بن رشيد عن محمد عبد قطع رجل يده ثم مات ثم اختلف
And if the author had said: 'If a slave with two complete rights is killed, qisas is not executed until both (right-holders) come together' it would have been more appropriate and more concise. As for its being more appropriate, it is because it includes the slave whose person is bequeathed to one person and his services to another, and others. Our saying 'two rights' is to indicate that if they are both owners, then their coming together is necessary. And its being more concise is evident. Our saying 'complete' is to exclude the sold slave killed before delivery, as will come. And in Fatawa al-Fadli: If the bequeathed (slave) is killed before the legatee accepts the bequest, there is no qisas for the heir nor for the legatee if they agree that he died before the legatee's acceptance; then afterward it is considered: if the legatee accepts the bequest, he recourse against the killer for his value, and the heirs do not recourse for that. And the slave whose person is bequeathed to a man and his services to another, if killed intentionally, there is no qisas unless they both come together. And in al-Kubra: If they agree, the right of the service-holder is nullified and the person-owner executes it; if the service-holder is not satisfied, then the value is owed by the killer and another slave is bought with it, and its condition is like the first. And in al-Quduri, Abu Yusuf said: The slave given as dower (mahr) if killed before the woman's possession, and the substitute for khul' if killed before the husband's possession, and the substitute for reconciliation for intentional blood if killed in the hand of the usurper intentionally, then if the owner wishes, he may execute qisas against the killer; if he wishes, he may hold the usurper liable for his slave's value, then the usurper recourse against the killer. And if the sold slave is killed before delivery, then qisas is for the buyer if he validates the sale because he is the owner; if he rescinds, then for the seller because the sale is annulled and he appears to be the owner, and this according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. And in al-Uyun and Fatawa al-Fadli: The sold slave if killed before delivery intentionally, the buyer has the choice between proceeding and returning; if he chooses to proceed, he may execute qisas, but he cannot execute it until after paying the price; they have permitted the validation of the sale after death here. If the buyer returns the sold item, the seller may execute qisas according to Abu Hanifa. If he pays the price, Abu Yusuf said: the seller does not execute qisas. And according to Muhammad, the value is obligatory in both cases due to confusion over the rightful claimant. And in Nawadir Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad: A man cut off the hand of another man's slave or wounded him, then the master sold him, then he returned him due to a defect by a judge's ruling, or the master gave him as a gift to someone, then revoked the gift by a judge's ruling or otherwise, then the slave died from the injury; then the slave's master recourse against the injurer for his full value. And in Nawadir Bishr from Abu Yusuf: If a female slave had her hand cut off by mistake and the master sold her to a person with an option, and she was returned to the master, then she died from the cut, then the cutter owes her full value; and if the cut was intentional, she sees qisas by istihsan. And in Nawadir Dawud ibn Rashid from Muhammad: A slave whose hand was cut by a man, then he died, then they differed...
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement where the author (possibly the commentator of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) suggests that the original text should have phrased the ruling more comprehensively: for any slave with 'two complete rights' (like ownership of person and services, or two co-owners), qisas requires the consent of both right-holders. He then lists various applications from fatwa collections and classical texts: a bequeathed slave before acceptance, a slave whose person and services are bequeathed separately, a slave given as dower, a substitute for khul', a slave in the hands of a usurper, a sold slave before delivery, and various narrations from Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on cases where the slave is injured then sold or gifted, then dies. The common thread is that whenever a slave has multiple right-holders (due to sale, pledge, bequest, etc.), qisas or compensation is subject to the agreement of all parties or specific rules to avoid loss to any right-holder.

Key Terms
حقان تمامان (haqqan tamaman): Two complete rights; meaning two separate claims over the same slave, such as ownership of the person and ownership of services.
موصى به (musa bihi): A bequeathed slave; the subject of a will, either the person or the services.
بدل الخلع (badal al-khul'): The compensation given by the wife in a khul' divorce; if a slave is given as compensation and killed before delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Guardianship in Qisas: Father, Executor, and Judge
Commentary on the powers of father, executor, and judge regarding qisas settlement for the insane and minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the powers of the father, executor, and judge regarding qisas and settlement for an insane or minor victim, and identify which guardian can execute or settle and under what conditions.

Core Idea
The father has full guardianship over the soul for qisas; the executor can only settle; the judge stands in the father's place.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the authority of guardians over qisas for the insane and minor. It clarifies that the father can execute qisas and settle for blood money, the executor can only settle (and for limbs by istihsan), and the judge has the same power as the father for the ward without a guardian. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the law of qisas when the victim lacks legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three guardians
List the father, executor, and judge as the guardians discussed. Note that the minor (sabi) is equated to the insane (ma'tuh) in terms of guardianship.
2
Determine the father's powers
The father has full authority over the soul: he can execute qisas or settle for blood money. He cannot pardon without compensation because that would nullify the right.
The text analogizes to marriage guardianship.
3
Determine the executor's powers
The executor can settle for money but cannot execute qisas. For limbs, the original riwaya denies him, but al-Jami' al-Saghir and istihsan grant it.
Disagreement within the school.
4
Determine the judge's powers
The judge is like the father in the correct view: he can execute qisas for a minor with no guardian. This is the later scholars' opinion.
Al-Natiqi disagreed.
5
Apply the analysis to a scenario
Given a specific case (e.g., insane victim's hand cut off intentionally), determine which guardian can act and whether qisas or settlement is permissible.
Use the rules and the istihsan for limbs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالأب والوصي يصالح فقط والصبي كالمعتوه. قال وله أن يصالح من غير قيد بقدر الدية فينبغي أن يجوز الصلح على أقل من الدية عملاً بإطلاقه. وإنما جاز صلحه على المال لأنه أنفع للمعتوه من القصاص فإذا جاز استيفاء القصاص فالصلح أولى، والنفع يحصل بالقليل والكثير ألا ترى أن الكرخي قال في مختصره : وإذا وجب لرجل على رجل قصاص في نفس أو فيما دونها فصالح صاحب الحق من على مال فذلك جائز ، قليلاً كان المال أو نظائر كثيرة في مسائل الفقه والله تعالى أعلم. أما القتل فلأن القصاص شرع للتشفي ودرك الثأر وكل ذلك راجع إلى النفس بولايته ولاية على نفسه فيليه كالإنكاح بخلاف الأخ وأمثاله حيث لا يكون لهم استيفاء قصاص وجب للمعتوه لأن الأب لوفور شفقته جعل التشفي الحاصل للابن ولهذا يعد ضرر ولده ضراً على نفسه، وأما العفو فلا يصح لأنه إبطال لحقه بلا عوض ولا مصلحة فلا يجوز، وكذلك إن قطعت يد المعتوه عمداً لما بينا. والوصي كالأب في جميع ما ذكرنا إلا في القتل فإنه لا يقتل لأن القتل من باب الولاية على النفس حتى لا يملك تزويجه، ويدخل تحت هذا الإطلاق الصلح عن النفس واستيفاء القصاص في الطرف إذا لم يسر القود في النفس. وذكر في كتاب الصلح أن الوصي لا يملك الصلح في النفس لأنه فيها بمنزلة الاستيفاء وهو لا يملك الاستيفاء. وجه المذكور هنا وهو المذكور في الجامع الصغير أن المقصود من الصلح المال والوصي يتولى التصرف فيه كما يتولى الأب بخلاف القصاص لأن القصد التشفي وهو مختص بالأب ولا يملك العفو لأن الأب لا يملكه في النفس لأن المقصود متحد وهو التشفي وفي الاستحسان يملكه لأن الأطراف يسلك فيها مسلك الأموال لأنها خلقت وقاية للأنفس كالمال فكان استيفاؤه بمنزلة التصرف فيه . والقاضي بمنزلة الأب فيه في الصحيح ألا ترى أن من قتل ولا ولي له يستوفيه السلطان والقاضي بمنزلته فيه وهذا ،أولى والصبي كالمعتوه لما عرف في موضعه. قال رحمه الله : (والقاضي كالأب والوصي يصالح فقط والصبي كالمعتوه) يعني أن القاضي يملك استيفاء القصاص في الصغير الذي لا ولي له وهو قول المتأخرين من أصحابنا. وذكر الناطقي أنه لا يملك والوصي يملك الصلح ولا يملك استيفاء القصاص هذا الكلام فيما إذا كان المجني عليه مولى الصغير أو المعتوه ، فلو جنى صغير أو مجنون على نفس أو طرف وأراد الأب أن يصالح عن ذلك فله ذلك . وقوله والوصي يصالح فقط هذا إذا كان القصاص في ولي المعتوه يملكه.
Like the father and executor: he only settles, and the minor is like the insane. He said: And he may settle without restriction to the amount of the blood money, so it should be permissible to settle for less than the blood money, acting upon its absoluteness. And his settlement for money is only allowed because it is more beneficial for the insane than qisas; so if it is permissible to execute qisas, then settlement is more appropriate, and benefit is obtained with little and much. Do you not see that al-Karkhi said in his Mukhtasar: If a person has a right of qisas against another for a life or less, and the claimant settles for money, that is permissible, whether the money is little or much, whether it is less than the blood money for life or the compensation for injury or more; up to here is the wording of the author of al-`Inaya. I say: His view is very weak, for the scholars of takhrij among the shaykhs restrict the absolute statement of the mujtahid when fiqh requires it, as they have stated, and there are many similar examples in fiqh issues, and Allah knows best. As for killing, because qisas is legislated for gratification and exacting revenge, and all that refers to the soul, by his guardianship he has authority over himself, so he acts like in marriage, unlike the brother and his likes, where they do not have the execution of qisas that is obligatory for the insane, because the father, due to his abundant compassion, the gratification obtained for the son is considered, and for this reason he considers harm to his son as harm to himself. As for pardon, it is not valid because it is an annulment of his right without compensation or benefit, so it is not permissible. Similarly, if the hand of the insane is cut off intentionally, due to what we have explained. And the executor is like the father in all that we have mentioned, except in killing, for he does not kill, because killing falls under guardianship over the soul, such that he cannot marry him off. And included under this absolute are settlement for the soul and execution of qisas for a limb if it does not lead to execution for the soul. And it is mentioned in the Book of Settlement that the executor does not have the authority to settle for the soul because it is in the position of execution, and he does not have execution. The reasoning mentioned here, which is mentioned in al-Jami` al-Saghir, is that the purpose of settlement is money, and the executor handles it as the father does, unlike qisas because the intent is gratification, which is specific to the father, and he does not have pardon because the father does not have it for the soul, because the purpose is unified, namely gratification. And by istihsan he has it, because limbs are treated like property, because they are created as a protection for the soul like wealth, so their execution is like disposing of wealth. And the judge is in the position of the father in this, according to the correct view. Do you not see that if someone is killed and has no guardian, the ruler executes, and the judge is in his position, and this is more appropriate. And the minor is like the insane, as is known in its place. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the judge is like the father and executor, he only settles, and the minor is like the insane), meaning that the judge has the authority to execute qisas for the minor who has no guardian, and this is the view of the later scholars among our fellows. And al-Natiqi mentioned that he does not have it, and the executor has settlement but not execution of qisas. This statement applies when the victim is under the guardianship of the minor or insane. So if a minor or insane person commits an offense against life or limb and the father wants to settle for it, he may do so. And his statement 'and the executor only settles' applies when qisas is for the soul; but if it is for limbs, then in the narration of the original he does not have it, and according to the narration of al-Jami` al-Saghir he has it. And Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that he has it by way of istihsan. And his statement 'and the minor is like the insane' means the guardian of the minor has what we have stated that the guardian of the insane has.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on a matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the authority of guardians (father, executor, judge) regarding qisas and settlement for an insane person (ma'tuh) and by extension a minor (sabi). The key ruling: the father and executor can only settle (sulh) for blood money, but cannot pardon without compensation. The father can execute qisas for his insane son's murder because he has guardianship over the son's soul and the gratification of vengeance reverts to him; he is like the father in marriage. The executor is like the father except he cannot execute the death penalty because that requires complete guardianship over the soul, which he lacks. There is a disagreement recorded: in the Book of Settlement, the executor cannot settle for the soul because it is like execution; but in al-Jami' al-Saghir, settlement is allowed because the purpose is money, and the executor handles money. For limbs, by istihsan the executor can execute qisas as they are treated like property. The judge is like the father in the correct view, having the authority to execute qisas for a minor without a guardian. The minor is equated to the insane in these rulings. The commentator also critiques the unrestricted statement of al-Karkhi, noting that later scholars restrict it when necessary.

Key Terms
الأب (al-ab): The father of the insane or minor victim; he has full guardianship over the soul for qisas and can execute or settle.
الوصي (al-wasi): The executor appointed by the father or judge; he can settle for money but cannot execute qisas in murder cases.
الصلح (al-sulh): Settlement where the victim's guardian accepts blood money in lieu of qisas.
المعتوه (al-ma'tuh): An insane person who is not legally responsible; his guardian acts on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the father and the executor regarding qisas for murder?
A) The father can execute qisas, but the executor cannot.
B) The father can settle for money, but the executor cannot.
C) The executor can pardon without compensation, but the father cannot.
D) The father can only settle, but the executor can execute qisas.

Key Takeaway
The father has the widest authority over qisas; the executor is limited to settlement; the judge stands in for the father for the ward without a guardian.

Qiyas vs Istihsan: Killer Who Becomes Insane
Ruling of Imam Muhammad: qiyas says execution, istihsan says blood money.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prefers istihsan over qiyas in the case of a killer who becomes insane after murder, and derive the resulting ruling.

Core Idea
When the killer loses sanity after murder, qiyas demands execution but istihsan substitutes blood money.

This short passage from al-Uyun presents a classic conflict between qiyas (analogical reasoning) and istihsan (juristic preference). Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani shows that strict analogy leads to execution, but the Hanafi school ultimately adopts istihsan, requiring blood money instead. You will examine the reasoning behind each approach and understand how Hanafi usul resolves such tensions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage presents two possible rulings for the same case: by qiyas the killer is executed, by istihsan blood money is taken from his property.
Note that the killer was sane at the time of murder but insane at the time of execution.
2
Analyze the qiyas argument
Qiyas argues that since the killer was legally responsible (mukallaf) when he committed the murder, the liability for qisas (retribution) is established and remains even if he later becomes insane.
The illa (effective cause) is intentional killing by a sane person at the time of the act.
3
Understand the istihsan override
Istihsan considers that executing an insane person does not serve the purpose of retribution, as he lacks awareness. Moreover, his insanity may be a result of guilt or trauma. Hence, blood money is preferred.
Istihsan overrides qiyas when qiyas leads to hardship or goes against the spirit of the law.
4
Trace the Hanafi principle
The Hanafi principle is that istihsan is a valid source of law that can restrict a qiyas ruling. Here, the ruling shifts from execution to blood money.
This illustrates the flexibility within Hanafi methodology.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Although the text does not explicitly state which is mufta bihi, the Hanafi school relies on the istihsan position, as it is consistent with the magnanimity of Islamic law.
The authority weight is nawadir (rare transmission), but the principle is well-established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي العيون: إذا ثبت القتل عليه ثم جنى القاتل قال محمد في القياس يقتل وفي الاستحسان تؤخذ منه الدية .
And in al-Uyun: If the murder is established against him and then the killer becomes insane, Muhammad said: by qiyas he is executed, and by istihsan blood money is taken from him.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This is a short but important ruling from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, passed in al-Uyun. It presents a conflict between strict analogy (qiyas) and juristic preference (istihsan). The qiyas: the killer committed murder while sane, so he deserves execution regardless of his later insanity (as he was accountable at the time of the act). But istihsan shifts to blood money because the killer's insanity at the time of execution removes his legal capacity, and executing an insane person does not serve the purpose of qisas (retribution) since he lacks awareness. Also, his insanity might have been caused by the crime (e.g., guilt). Thus the Hanafi school prefers the istihsan position: take blood money from his property. This illustrates the principle that when qiyas leads to hardship or goes against public interest, istihsan can override it.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning that demands execution because the killer was sane at the time of the crime.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that leads to taking blood money instead of execution due to the killer's insanity.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money paid to the victim's heirs in lieu of qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He is executed anyway.
B) He is set free.
C) Blood money is taken from him.
D) He is imprisoned until he recovers.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a killer becomes insane after conviction, istihsan overrides qiyas and blood money is taken instead of execution.

Qisas — Retaliation

Adult Heirs and Qisas: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Waiting for Minors
Intra-Hanafi dispute on immediate qisas by adult heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position on immediate qisas by adult heirs, explain the Sahibayn's objection, and evaluate the strength of the precedent of Ali as a proof.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa allows adult heirs to execute qisas immediately; the Sahibayn require waiting until minors mature.

When a murder victim leaves both adult and minor heirs, a critical question arises: can the adults demand qisas (retaliation) before the minors reach puberty? The Hanafi school is divided. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and his two distinguished students, the precedent from the time of Sayyiduna Ali, and the subtle analysis of the indivisibility of qisas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute's core question
The passage begins with the matn ruling that adults may take qisas before minors mature (Abu Hanifa's view). Your first task is to pinpoint the exact legal question: may adult heirs execute qisas when there are minor co-heirs who have not yet reached puberty?
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the matn reflects Abu Hanifa's view, but the Sahibayn dissent.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's objection
Next, isolate the argument of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. They assert that qisas is a shared right, and adults lack guardianship over minors' shares. Delay is therefore necessary, analogizing to cases where an adult heir is absent. Note the thamarah al-khilaf: under their view, execution is postponed until minors can consent.
The Sahibayn also distinguish pardon: an adult can pardon but it voids the minor's right only with compensation (diyah).
3
Evaluate Abu Hanifa's proof from the precedent of Ali
Study the central proof: when Abd al-Rahman ibn Muljam killed Ali, his adult son executed him even though Ali had minor children. This occurred in the presence of the Companions without objection, thus constituting tacit consensus. How does this precedent override the Sahibayn's analogy?
Abu Hanifa argues that the cause (kinship) is indivisible, making qisas itself indivisible. Any one heir can execute it on behalf of all.
4
Follow the commentator's critique of the indivisibility reasoning
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) challenges the causal logic: just because kinship is indivisible does not necessarily mean the right to qisas is indivisible. He gives the counterexample of blood money (diyah) — also caused by kinship — which is clearly divisible. Then he endorses the analysis that the act of killing itself is indivisible, a stronger basis.
This critique shows the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning; the student should appreciate that the school's internal debates refine the doctrine.
5
Distinguish consensus cases and apply the rulings
Finally, note the exceptions: if the adult heir is the father or grandfather, there is consensus that he can execute qisas or take full blood money due to his guardianship. If the adult is a brother or uncle without guardianship, he can only take his own share. The passage also considers accidental killing scenarios. Apply this distinction to hypothetical scenarios.
This step consolidates the practical outcome of the dispute.

What he said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And for the adults, retaliation before the minors reach adulthood) meaning if the retaliation is shared, such that a man is killed and he has adult and minor children, then the adults may retaliate against the killer before the minors reach puberty. This is according to Abu Hanifa. But the two companions said: They do not have that until the minors reach puberty, because retaliation is shared among them, and because the adults have no guardianship over the minors to execute their right, so delay is determined, just as if all were adults and there was an absent adult, or if one of the two guardians was absent in a jointly owned slave, unlike when an adult pardons, where his pardon is valid, even though the minor's right to retaliation becomes void, but it is voided with compensation, so it is like both are voided. And for Abu Hanifa, it is narrated that when Abd al-Rahman ibn Muljam killed Ali, he was executed for it, and among Ali's children were minors, and this happened in the presence of the Companions without any objection, so it became like consensus. And for this reason, if some of the guardians execute the killing themselves, they are not liable for anything, and if they did not have that, they would be liable, like if a stranger killed the person subject to retaliation, so they differ. And unlike the case when there are two guardians and one is a minor, because the cause of ownership or wala is incomplete, whereas in our case, kinship is complete. The commentator said: And because it is a right that cannot be divided, because its cause, which is kinship, is indivisible. I say: In the complete reasoning by the indivisibility of the cause of retaliation, which is kinship, to prove the indivisibility of retaliation itself, there is some obscurity, because the intellect does not find a problem in the cause being simple and the effect complex. Moreover, it is apparent that kinship, which is indivisible, just as it is the cause for entitlement to retaliation for intentional killing, it is also the cause for entitlement to blood money for accidental killing, even though there is no doubt that blood money is divisible because it is wealth, and wealth is divisible without doubt. So the most apparent in explaining that retaliation is an indivisible right is what is mentioned in al-Kafi and Mi'raj al-Diraya: the statement of the two imams' evidence: that killing is indivisible. Then some virtuous person criticized their statement here that the cause of retaliation is kinship, saying: How can its cause be kinship when it is established for the husband and wife? I say: Yes, the cause for the husband and wife is marriage, and in emancipation and the manumitted, it is wala, not kinship. But the apparent meaning of their statement here, 'kinship', is either based on predominance because the majority of the heirs of the victim are relatives, or based on that they intended by 'kinship' here the connection that necessitates inheritance, not literal kinship, so it includes all. And we restricted the place of disagreement to when retaliation is between brothers; so if it is between the father and minor children, or between the grandfather and minor children, then the father and grandfather may execute retaliation by consensus. And in al-Jami': This issue has two aspects: either the killing is intentional or accidental. If accidental, then if the adult partner is the father of the minor, he may take the entire blood money: his own share by right of ownership and the minor's share by right of guardianship. If the adult partner is a brother or uncle and is not a guardian of the minor, he takes his own share but does not take the minor's share. If the killing is intentional, if the adult partner is the father, he may execute retaliation by consensus. If the adult partner is a stranger, such as when a slave is killed and he is jointly owned by two strangers
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a key intra-Hanafi dispute on whether adult heirs can demand qisas immediately when some heirs are minors. The matn states that adults can take retaliation before minors reach puberty, which is the view of Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, arguing that qisas is a shared right and adults have no guardianship over minors' rights, so they must wait. Abu Hanifa's proof is the historical precedent of Ali ibn Abi Talib: when he was killed by Ibn Muljam, his adult son (probably Hasan) executed him, even though there were minor children (Husayn was a minor? Actually both were adults? Wait, the text says 'in the presence of the Companions without objection' implying consensus). Abu Hanifa argues that qisas is an indivisible right because its cause (kinship) is indivisible, so any one heir can execute it on behalf of all. The commentator then critiques this reasoning, noting that the cause (kinship) being simple does not necessarily make the effect (qisas) indivisible, as seen with blood money which is divisible despite the same cause. He prefers the analysis that the act of killing itself is indivisible. The passage also distinguishes cases: if the adult heir is the father or grandfather, there is consensus he can execute qisas or take full blood money due to his guardianship. If the adult is a brother or uncle without guardianship, he can only take his own share. The discussion includes a critique of using 'kinship' as the cause for qisas, since husband/wife are heirs without blood kinship; the commentator explains it is either a generalization or intended as 'connection leading to inheritance'.

Key Terms
القود (al-qawd): Retaliation or qisas, specifically execution of the killer.
الكبار (al-kibar): Adult heirs who have reached puberty.
الصغار (al-sighar): Minor heirs who have not yet reached puberty.
القرابة (al-qarabah): Kinship as the cause for inheritance and succession in qisas, broadly including marriage and wala.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa allows immediate qisas by adult heirs based on the precedent of Ali and the indivisibility of the right; the Sahibayn require waiting for minors, but the father/grandfather is an exception by consensus.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate

Qisas with Adult and Minor Heirs: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether adult heirs can execute qisas before minor heirs reach puberty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on immediate qisas execution when heirs include minors and evaluate the reasoning of Abu Hanifa versus the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
An adult heir may execute qisas immediately even if minor heirs exist, because the killing itself is indivisible.

When a person is killed and his heirs include both adults and minors, a critical question arises: can the adult heirs execute the killer (qisas) before the minors reach puberty? This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi dispute on this issue. Imam Abu Hanifa permits immediate execution, while his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) require waiting. We will analyse their proofs, the underlying principle of indivisibility of the right to qisas, and the practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize the factual scenario: a man killed leaving adult and minor children. The question is whether adults may execute qisas before minors reach puberty.
The right to qisas is shared among all heirs.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa permits immediate execution. His proof is the historical precedent of Ibn Muljam, executed for killing Ali despite Ali's minor children, with no Companion objection – establishing consensus.
This is a dalil naqli (narrative evidence).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require waiting until minors reach puberty. Their proof: qisas is a shared right, and adults have no guardianship over minors; analogously, if all were adults and one absent, the present cannot execute alone.
They also note that pardon by an adult is valid even if it nullifies the minor's share because of compensation.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response and the nature of the right
The text argues that the right to qisas is indivisible because its cause (kinship) is indivisible, or more soundly, because the killing itself is indivisible. Thus an adult's execution covers the entire right. The adult does not guarantee damages, unlike a stranger who executes.
The commentary refines the reasoning: indivisibility is based on the act of killing, not the cause.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: immediate execution vs. delay. The mufta bihi (operative view) is Abu Hanifa's, supported by the consensus-based precedent and the majority of later Hanafi scholars.
However, the Sahibayn's view is also recorded and applied in certain contexts.
