Meaning: The guardian of a minor possesses what we mentioned regarding the guardian of an insane person. In al-Uyun: If the killing is established against him, then the killer commits another crime, Muhammad said in qiyas he is killed, and in istihsan the diya is taken from him. What the author said: 'And for the adults is the qisas before the minors reach puberty' means when the qisas is shared, such that a man is killed and he has adult and minor children, then the adults may execute the killer before the minors reach puberty, and this is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: They do not have that until the minors reach puberty, because qisas is shared among them, and because the adults have no guardianship over the minors to execute their right, so delay is necessary, just as if all were adults and one adult was absent, or one of the two guardians was absent in the case of a shared slave, unlike when the adult pardons where his pardon is valid, and even if the minor's right to qisas is invalidated, it is invalidated with compensation, so it is like non-invalidation. And for Abu Hanifa, it is reported that Abd al-Rahman ibn Muljam when he killed Ali was killed for it, and among Ali's children were minors, and this was in the presence of the Companions without any objection, so it became like consensus. For this reason, if one of the guardians executes the killing himself, he does not guarantee anything; if it were not so, he would guarantee, just as if a stranger kills the one against whom qisas is due. So they differ. And unlike the case where there are two owners and one is a minor, because the cause of ownership or wala' is not complete; in our case, the kinship is complete. The commentator said: Because it is a right that cannot be divided, because its cause—kinship—cannot be divided. I say: In the complete inference of the indivisibility of the cause of qisas, which is kinship, upon the indivisibility of qisas itself, there is obscurity, because the intellect does not find a problem in the cause being simple and the effect being composite. How is it apparent that kinship, which is indivisible, is the cause for the entitlement of qisas in intentional killing, and also for the entitlement of diya in accidental killing, while there is no doubt that diya is divisible because it is property, and property is undoubtedly divisible? So the more apparent in explaining that qisas is an indivisible right is what is mentioned in al-Kafi and Miraj al-Diraya as the presentation of the proof of the two Imams, which is that killing is indivisible. Then some virtuous scholars objected to their statement here that the cause of qisas is kinship, saying: How can its cause be kinship while it is established for the husband and wife? End. I say: Yes, the cause for the husband and wife is marriage; for the emancipated and the female emancipated, it is wala, not kinship. Except that the apparent meaning of their statement 'kinship' here is either based on predominance (since most heirs in killing are relatives by kinship) or based on their intending by 'kinship' here the connection that causes inheritance, not the reality of kinship, so it includes all. And we restricted the subject of dispute to qisas between two brothers. So if it is between the father and young children, or between the grandfather and young children, then the father and grandfather may execute qisas by consensus. In al-Jami: This issue has two aspects: either the killing is intentional or accidental. If it is accidental, then if the adult partner is the father of the minor, he may collect the entire diya—his own share by ownership and the minor's share by guardianship. If the adult partner is a brother or uncle and not a guardian of the minor, he collects his own share but does not collect the minor's share. If the killing is intentional, then if the adult partner is the father, he may execute qisas by consensus. If the adult partner is a stranger, such that a slave is killed and he is shared between two strangers, likewise it is the cause. And in al-Muntaqa: Unless the minor is a son, then he may execute at that time. And if the adult partner is a brother or uncle, then according to Abu Hanifa he may execute qisas before the minor reaches puberty; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he may not do so until the minor reaches puberty. And on this difference, if the adult partner is an idiot or insane, and the adult is the brother of the idiot or his uncle, and the ruler wants to execute the minor's share with the adult, there is no doubt that according to Abu Hanifa he may do so. And according to the two, he may not. And they agreed that if the qisas is entirely for the minor, the adult brother has no authority to execute. And regarding a slave shared between a minor and an adult who is killed intentionally such that qisas becomes due, and the adult wants to execute the qisas, some of our teachers said it is subject to the difference, and some said the adult does not execute it by consensus. And regarding a man who has two slaves and one kills the other intentionally, the heir (wali) may execute qisas from the killer. Muhammad mentioned this at the end of the chapter on freeing slaves in the section on injuries of slaves.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage opens an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the execution of qisas when the heirs include both adults and minors. The base text states that if a man is killed and his children include adults and minors, the adult children have the right to execute the killer before the minors reach puberty. The author attributes this ruling to Imam Abu Hanifa. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree: they maintain that the adults must wait until the minors reach puberty. Their reasoning is that qisas is a shared right, and adults have no guardianship over minors to claim their share; therefore delay is necessary. They analogize this to a case where all heirs are adults but one is absent, or where a shared slave has two owners and one is absent—in such cases the present owner cannot unilaterally execute. However, they make an exception for pardon: if an adult pardons, it is valid, even though the minor's right to qisas is lost, because it is lost with compensation. Imam Abu Hanifa supports his view by citing the historical precedent of Abd al-Rahman ibn Muljam, who was executed for assassinating Caliph Ali despite some of Ali's children being minors. This incident occurred in the presence of the Companions without objection, effectively establishing consensus. Furthermore, if one heir executes qisas himself, he is not liable for damages; otherwise he would be, as in the case of a stranger executing. The commentator then discusses the nature of qisas as an indivisible right, arguing that its cause—kinship—is indivisible. However, the author of the Sharh critiques this reasoning, noting that the cause can be simple while the effect is composite. He illustrates with diya, which is divisible even though its cause (kinship) is not. The correct reasoning, according to al-Kafi and Miraj al-Diraya, is that killing itself is indivisible. The passage also addresses objections: some raised that the cause of qisas cannot be kinship because it is also established for spouses and emancipated slaves. The response is that for spouses the cause is marriage, and for slaves it is wala', not kinship; the term 'kinship' here is used broadly to refer to the connection that causes inheritance, which includes these relationships. The dispute is then restricted to cases where the adult and minor heirs are brothers; when the adult is the father or grandfather, he may execute qisas by consensus. The passage further details scenarios of accidental and intentional killing, and the rights of guardians in each. It concludes with additional cases: shared slaves and multiple slaves, noting where consensus exists.

Key Terms
قود (qawd): Execution of the killer by the heirs (qisas) in retaliation for intentional killing.
ولي (wali): The legal guardian or heir entitled to execute qisas or receive diya.
قرابة (qarabah): Kinship; here the cause of entitlement to qisas, extended to include relationships causing inheritance (like marriage and wala').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, can adult children execute qisas before minor children reach puberty?
A) Yes, based on the incident of Ibn Muljam
B) No, they must wait until the minors reach puberty
C) Only if the minors are sons
D) Only if the adult is the father

Key Takeaway
Immediate execution of qisas by adult heirs is valid in the Hanafi school, based on the precedent of Ibn Muljam and the indivisibility of the right.

Killing with a Tool (Mar): Condition for Qisas
Ruling on qisas with the tool 'mar'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for qisas when a tool (mar) is used, and apply it to distinguish between different modes of striking.

Core Idea
Qisas with a tool requires the iron edge to strike — the sharp part, not the handle.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a specific ruling on qisas: when a person is killed using a digging tool called 'mar', the applicability of qisas depends on which part of the tool made contact. The text distinguishes between the iron cutting edge and the wooden back/handle, aligning with general Hanafi principles about instruments that typically cause death by sharpness versus blunt force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is killing with a specific tool (mar). The ruling is conditional: qisas is wajib if the iron part strikes, otherwise not.
The ruling is from the matn, so it is authoritative (zahir al-riwayah).
2
Parse the key terms and condition
The condition is that the victim is struck by the 'hadid' (iron, i.e., the sharp edge). 'Mar' is defined as a long stick with an iron head, used for digging.
The specific tool is not essential; the principle applies to any similar tool with a sharp iron part.
3
Apply the condition to the two scenarios
Scenario 1: strike with the iron edge → qisas applies, no dispute. Scenario 2: strike with the back of the iron or the wood → like strangulation/drowning, no qisas, but subject to a prior Hanafi dispute (whether killing with a heavy object entails qisas).
The prior dispute is mentioned at the beginning of the chapter; the mufta bihi is likely that qisas does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن قتله بمر يقتص أن أصابه الحديد وإلا لا كالخنق والتغريق) هذا إذا أصابه بحد الحديد من غير خلاف، وإن أصابه بظهرها أو بالعود لا كالخنق والتغريق فهو على الخلاف الذي ذكرناه في أول الباب. والمرعود في طرفها حديدة قال العيني: المر بفتح الميم وتشديد الراء وهو خشبة طويلة في رأسها حديدة عريضة من فوقها خشبة عريضة يضع الرجل رجله عليها ويحفر بها الأرض، وبالفارسية تسمى بيل قال.
The author said: 'And if he kills him with a tool (mar), qisas applies if the iron hits him, otherwise not, like strangulation and drowning.' This is if he hits him with the iron edge without dispute. And if he hits him with its back or the wood, not like strangulation and drowning, then it is subject to the difference we mentioned at the beginning of the chapter. And the mar is a tool with iron at its tip. Al-Aini said: 'Mar' is with a fatha on the meem and tashdid on the ra, and it is a long stick with a wide iron at its head, and on top of it a wide piece of wood on which a man places his foot and digs the ground with it. In Persian it is called bil.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a ruling regarding a specific tool called 'mar' (a spade-like instrument). The author of the matn states that if a person kills another with this tool, qisas is applicable only if the iron part of the tool strikes the victim. If the strike is with the wooden back or the handle, it is not subject to qisas, similar to killing by strangulation or drowning, which do not entail qisas. The commentator explains that if the blow is with the iron edge (the cutting part), there is no dispute that qisas is due. However, if the blow is with the back of the iron or the wooden part, the ruling follows the earlier disagreement from the beginning of the chapter regarding killing with a heavy object. The term 'mar' is then defined by al-Aini: it is a long stick with a wide piece of iron at the tip, and a wide wooden piece above it for the foot, used for digging. In Persian, it is called 'bil' (spade). The key distinction is whether the instrument is used in a manner that typically kills (with a sharp edge) or not.

Key Terms
مر (mar): A long-handled tool with an iron head, used for digging; spade.
حديد (hadid): Iron; specifically the cutting edge of the tool.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a person is killed with a mar, when is qisas applicable?
A) Whenever the tool strikes the victim.
B) Only if the iron part strikes the victim.
C) Only if the strike is fatal.
D) Only if the tool is made entirely of iron.

Key Takeaway
For qisas with a tool, the Hanafi condition is that the sharp (iron) part must strike, not any other part.

Killing a Sword-Brandisher: Wajib, No Liability
States ruling and proof for killing a sword-brandisher

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing a sword-brandisher and identify the hadith proof and the condition of necessity.

Core Idea
Brandishing a sword against Muslims makes one's blood lawful; killing is wajib, and the killer bears no liability.

This lesson examines a critical ruling in Hanafi fiqh concerning self-defense and public security. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the situation where a person openly threatens Muslims with a weapon. The ruling establishes that such an aggressor becomes a rebel (baghi) and his life loses its protected status. We will explore the textual proof from the hadith, the underlying principle of repelling harm, and the conditions under which lethal force is justified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is brandishing a sword against Muslims. The ruling is that killing such a person is obligatory (wajib) and the killer is not liable for any compensation or qisas.
The ruling applies to any weapon, not just a sword.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Whoever brandishes a sword against Muslims has made his blood lawful.' This is a sunnah ahad, but accepted as proof.
The hadith directly indicates that the blood of the aggressor is no longer protected.
3
Apply the principle of repelling harm
The underlying principle is that repelling harm (darar) is obligatory. If killing is the only way to repel the harm, then killing becomes obligatory.
This is a qiyas based on the general rule of necessity.
4
Determine the condition of necessity
Killing is only justified if the harm cannot be repelled otherwise. The passage distinguishes between situations where help is available (daytime in the city) and where it is not (night or outside the city).
If help is available, lethal force may not be justified.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
In al-Nawadir, there is a difference regarding whether the killed aggressor should be washed and prayed over. One narration says yes, another says washed but not prayed upon.
This does not affect the core ruling of wajib killing and no liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أشهر على المسلمين سيفاً وجب قتله ولا شيء بقتله لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «من شهر على المسلمين سيفاً فقد أبطل دمه ولأن دفع الضرر واجب فوجب عليهم قتله إذا لم يكن دفعه إلا به، ولا يجب على القاتل شيء لأنه صار باغياً بذلك، وكذا إذا أشهر على رجل سلاحاً فقتله أو قتله غيره دفعاً عنه فلا يجب بقتله شيء لما بينا، ولا يختلف بين أن يكون بالليل أو بالنهار في المصر أو خارج المصر لأنه لا يلحقه الغوث بالليل ولا في خارج المصر فكان له دفعة بالقتل بخلاف ما إذا كان في المصر نهاراً. وفي النوادر : يغسل ويصلى عليه، وعن الثاني يغسل ولا يصلى عليه
And whoever brandishes a sword against Muslims, his killing is obligatory, and there is no liability for killing him, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever brandishes a sword against Muslims has made his blood lawful.' And because repelling harm is obligatory, so it is obligatory upon them to kill him if his repulsion cannot be achieved except by that. And nothing is incumbent upon the killer because he has become a rebel (baghi) thereby. Similarly, if he brandishes a weapon against a man and kills him, or someone else kills him in defense, nothing is incumbent for killing him due to what we have stated. And there is no difference between night and day, or within the city or outside it, because he cannot get help at night or outside the city, so he may repel him by killing, contrary to if it is daytime within the city. And in al-Nawadir: he is washed and prayed upon, and from the second narration: he is washed but not prayed upon.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage states the ruling regarding a person who brandishes a sword against Muslims. The ruling is that it is obligatory to kill such a person, and the killer is not liable for any compensation or qisas. The proof is the hadith: 'Whoever brandishes a sword against Muslims has made his blood lawful.' Additionally, the reasoning is that repelling harm is obligatory, and if the only way to repel the harm is by killing the aggressor, then killing is obligatory. The aggressor is considered a rebel (baghi), so his blood is not protected. This applies whether the brandishing is against all Muslims or against a specific individual, and whether the killing is done by the intended victim or by someone else defending him. The ruling does not differ between night and day, or inside or outside the city, because outside the city or at night, the person cannot get help quickly, so lethal defense is permitted. However, if it is inside the city during the day, help is available, so killing might not be justified. The passage also mentions a difference of narration in al-Nawadir regarding whether the killed person should be washed and prayed over: one narration says yes, another says he is washed but not prayed upon.

Key Terms
شهر سيفاً (shahhara sayfan): To brandish a sword openly in a threatening manner against Muslims.
باغياً (baghiyan): A rebel or aggressor who commits transgression (baghy) and loses the protection of his life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal status of killing a person who brandishes a sword against Muslims?
A) It is haram, and the killer must pay diyah.
B) It is obligatory, and the killer is not liable.
C) It is permissible but the killer must pay compensation.
D) It is recommended, but the killer must make kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
Brandishing a sword against Muslims makes one's blood lawful; killing is wajib and the killer bears no liability, provided the harm cannot be repelled otherwise.

Self-Defense Killing: Brandishing Weapon vs Stick
Ruling with proof on self-defense and qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on self-defense killing when a stick is brandished in the city during the day and identify the factors that determine liability.

Core Idea
Threat with a stick in the city daytime triggers qisas because help is available.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced case in qisas: when a person brandishes a weapon or stick, and the threatened person kills him. The ruling depends on the type of object, time, location, and availability of help (ghawth). The passage also reveals an intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, and a later fatwa that shifts the ruling due to societal change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Note the opening ruling: brandishing a weapon (any time, any place) or a stick (at night or outside the city) leads to no liability for the killer. This is because help is presumed unavailable.
This covers most scenarios.
2
Spot the exception
The text then contrasts: brandishing a stick in the city during the day results in qisas (retaliation) for the killer. The reason given is that a stick is light and help is not cut off.
The exception is based on ghawth.
3
Trace the reasoning of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa holds that a stick is not like a weapon (silah). Since help is available, the killer becomes a transgressor (mu'tadi) and is liable for qisas. The stick's lightness makes it possible to seek help rather than kill.
Key distinction: stick vs weapon.
4
Examine the positions of Sahibayn and the later fatwa
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad also impose qisas but on the basis that a stick is like a weapon, making the killing intentional. However, a later opinion (qila) states that because people have abandoned helping, even a stick in the city daytime results in no liability.
The later fatwa is considered mufta bihi in later times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ومن شهر على رجل سلاحاً ليلاً أو نهاراً في المصر أو غيره أو شهر عليه عصا ليلاً أو نهاراً في غيره فقتله المشهور عليه فلا شيء عليه) لما بينا من المنقول والمعقول . قال رحمه الله : (ومن شهر عصا نهاراً في مصر فقتله المشهور عليه قتل به لأن العصا خفيفة والغوث غير منقطع في المصر فكان بالقتل معتدياً، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى ظاهر لأنه ليس كالسلاح عنده ، وقيل عندهما يحتمل أن يكون على الخلاف المذكور في العمد لأنه كالسلاح عندهما حتى يجب القصاص بالقتل به وقد بيناه، وقيل هذا في الزمان المتقدم، أما اليوم إذا شهر عليه العصا في مصر وقتله لا شيء عليه لأن الناس تركوا الإغاثة والغوث.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And whoever brandishes a weapon against a man, by night or day, in the city or elsewhere, or brandishes a stick against him by night or day elsewhere, and the threatened person kills him, there is no liability upon him.) Due to what we have stated from transmitted and rational proofs. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And whoever brandishes a stick by day in the city, and the threatened person kills him, he is killed for it (i.e., qisas) because the stick is light and help is not cut off in the city, so he becomes a transgressor by killing. And this is apparent according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, because it is not like a weapon according to him. And it is said according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that it is possible that it is subject to the previously mentioned difference of opinion regarding intentional killing, because it is like a weapon according to them, so qisas becomes obligatory by killing with it, and we have explained it. And it is said that this (the stricter view) was in former times; but nowadays, if he brandishes a stick against him in the city and kills him, there is no liability upon him because people have abandoned helping.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage first quotes the matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: 'Whoever brandishes a weapon against a man, by night or day, in the city or elsewhere, or brandishes a stick against him by night or day elsewhere, and the threatened person kills him, there is no liability upon him.' This means that in those situations, self-defense is justified because help is generally unavailable (at night or outside the city). Then the commentary adds: 'Whoever brandishes a stick by day in the city, and the threatened person kills him, he is killed for it (i.e., qisas).' The reason is that a stick is a light weapon, and in the city during the day, help is available, so the killing is considered transgression. According to Abu Hanifa, a stick is clearly not like a weapon, so qisas is due. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, there is a possibility that it is like a weapon based on the earlier discussion on intentional killing, in which case qisas is also due. However, a later opinion says that in current times, people have abandoned helping, so even with a stick in the city, no liability attaches.

Key Terms
عصا (asa): A stick, considered a light weapon in this context.
غوث (ghawth): Help or rescue that can be sought from others in times of danger.
معتدياً (mu'tadiyan): A transgressor, one who exceeds the limits of self-defense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling if someone brandishes a stick against a man in the city during the day and the man kills him?
A) No liability, because he was defending himself.
B) The killer must pay diyah.
C) The killer is subject to qisas.
D) The killer must only pay compensation for the stick.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on self-defense killing depends on the type of threat (weapon vs stick) and the availability of help (ghawth); Abu Hanifa holds that a stick in the city daytime does not justify killing because help is available, but later fatwas may adjust based on social conditions.

Qisas — Retaliation
intermediate
8 min
Killing an Attacker After Withdrawal
States that killing an attacker after withdrawal is murder, with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an attacker who has struck withdraws and the victim kills him, and explain why the attacker's inviolability is restored.

Core Idea
Once an attacker withdraws, his blood is no longer permissible – killing him becomes murder requiring qisas.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical nuance in the law of self-defense: the moment the threat ends, the attacker regains inviolability. While a person may defend himself against an imminent attack, the right to use lethal force expires the instant the attacker withdraws. Understanding this principle prevents the deadly escalation of a conflict beyond what is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the initial ruling.
The brandisher strikes and then withdraws; the victim kills him. The initial question: is the killer liable?
2
Note the key condition that changes the ruling.
The withdrawal negates the justification for killing. The text emphasizes that the attacker 'withdraws in a manner not intending a second strike'.
The condition is genuine withdrawal without intent to resume the attack.
3
Trace the reasoning: why does withdrawal restore inviolability?
Because the permissibility of the attacker's blood was contingent on the ongoing threat. Once the threat is removed (his evil is repelled), he returns to the protected status (ma'sum).
The Arabic phrase 'ada ma'suman' is central: he becomes inviolable again.
4
Apply the consequence: qisas becomes obligatory.
Killing a ma'sum person unjustly (without right) is deliberate murder (qatl 'amd) requiring retaliation (qisas).
The ruling is undisputed in the Hanafi school; it applies to all similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الله تعالى : (ولو ضربه الشاهر فانصرف فقتله الآخر قتل القاتل معناه إذا شهر رجل على رجل سلاحاً فضربه الشاهر فانصرف، ثم إن المضروب وهو المشهور عليه ضرب الضارب وهو الشاهر فقتله فعليه القصاص لأن الشاهر لما انصرف بعد الضرب عاد معصوماً مثل ما كان لأن حل دمه كان باعتبار شهره وضربه، فإذا رجع على وجه لا يريد ضربة ثانياً اندفع شره فلا حاجة إلى قتله لارتفاع شره بدونه فعادت عصمته فإذا قتله بعد ذلك فقد قتل رجلاً معصوماً ظلماً فيجب عليه القصاص.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if the brandisher strikes and then withdraws, and the other kills him, the killer is killed (qisas).) Its meaning: when a man brandishes a weapon against a man, and the brandisher strikes and then withdraws, then the struck person (the one against whom the weapon was brandished) strikes the striker (the brandisher) and kills him, then qisas is incumbent upon him. Because when the brandisher withdrew after the strike, he became inviolable again as before. Because the permissibility of his blood was based on his brandishing and striking. So when he retreated in a manner that he does not intend a second strike, his evil is repelled, and there is no need to kill him since his evil is removed without it. So his inviolability returns. So if he kills him after that, he has unjustly killed an inviolable person, so qisas is obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling when an attacker first strikes and then withdraws. The scenario: A person brandishes a weapon and strikes his target. Then he withdraws (i.e., stops the attack and moves away). After that, the original victim strikes the attacker and kills him. The ruling is that qisas is due on the killer. The reason is that the permissibility of killing the attacker was contingent upon his ongoing threat. Once he withdraws and does not intend a second strike, his evil is repelled without killing him. Therefore, his inviolability is restored. Killing him after withdrawal is unjust, and the killer is subject to qisas. This emphasizes that self-defense is only valid as long as the threat is imminent and ongoing.

Key Terms
انصرف (insarafa): To withdraw or turn away from the attack, indicating cessation of aggression.
عاد معصوماً (ada ma'suman): He returns to being protected (inviolable) after the threat ends.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man brandishes a sword and strikes his victim, then immediately turns and walks away. The victim then kills him. What is the ruling?
A) The victim has no liability because the attacker started it.
B) The victim must pay diyah.
C) The victim is subject to qisas.
D) The victim must only pay compensation for the sword.

Key Takeaway
Killing an attacker after he withdraws is unjustified and requires qisas because his inviolability is restored.

Qisas — Retaliation

Conditions for Qisas on Limbs and Body Parts
Chapter opening with general principle and detailed applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for wajib qisas on bodily injuries below the soul and apply them to specific body parts, distinguishing when compensation (arsh or hukuma) substitutes.

Core Idea
Qisas on limbs is wajib only when exact equivalence is possible at the joint; otherwise compensation applies.

This passage opens the chapter on qisas for injuries less than death. It lays down the central principle that retribution must be based on exact equivalence (mumathala) and can only be carried out on body parts with joints where precise matching is feasible. Through the hand, ear, nose, and penis, we learn where qisas applies, where it is replaced by arsh or hukuma, and what conditions must be met.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule and its Quranic proof
Identify the matn's ruling: qisas is performed by cutting from the joint, supported by Quran 5:45. Note the principle that qisas depends on mumathala (equivalence).
2
List the conditions for qisas on limbs
From the passage, extract conditions: cut at joint, same side (right for right, not right for left), same state (healthy for healthy, not healthy for paralyzed), same status (free for free, man for man, etc.). Also size does not matter because benefit is equal.
Condition of equivalence of benefit is key.
3
Identify when qisas is replaced by arsh or hukuma
When conditions are not met (e.g., cut not at joint, pulling instead of cutting, or the target limb is missing or deficient), qisas is not wajib; instead fixed compensation (arsh) or equitable compensation (hukumat 'adl) applies.
4
Apply the rule to specific body parts in the text
Work through the examples: hand, foot, ear (fatty part, cartilage, pulling), nose (tip vs. root), penis (from root or glans). For each, confirm whether qisas applies or not, and the reasoning.
Pay attention to the Nawadir narrations that add nuance for the ear.
5
Analyze the qiyas used for the penis case
Note the analogy: the penis has a known limit (glans), so it resembles the hand from the wrist; qisas is possible. This illustrates how qiyas fills gaps when the text is silent.
The qiyas is based on illa of having a known limit enabling exact equivalence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القصاص فيما دون النفس يقتص بقطع اليد من المفصل وإن كانت يد القاطع أكبر وكذا الرجل ومارن الأنف والأذن لقوله تعالى : والجروح قصاص [المائدة : ٤٥] أي ذو قصاص لقوله تعالى : والسن بالسن [المائدة : ٤٥] والقصاص ينبني على المماثلة فكل ما أمكن فيه رعاية المماثلة يجب فيه القصاص وما لا فلا. وقد أمكن في هذه الأشياء التي ذكرناها ولا عبرة بكبر العضو لأنه لا يوجب التفاوت في المنفعة، وإذا قلنا أن المدار عن التساوي في المنفعة فلا تقطع اليمنى باليسرى ولا الصحيحة بالشلاء، ولا يد المرأة بيد الرجل، ولا يد الحر بيد العبد. وقيد بقوله من المفصل لأنه لو قطع ذلك من غير المفصل لا قصاص فيه. وفي النوادر روى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة رحمهما الله تعالى أنه إذا قطع شحمه اذنه يقتص منه، وإن قطع نصف إذنه وكان يقدر أن يقتص مثل ذلك اقتص منه لأن شحمه الأذن لها حد معلوم وللأذن مفاصل معلومة، فإذا قطع منها شيء يعلم أن القطع من أي المفصل أمكن القصاص، وكذلك إذا قطع غضروف الأذن قطعاً يستطاع فيه القصاص اقتص منه يعمل ذلك بحديدة أو بغير حديدة. وإن جذب أذنه فانتزع شحمته لا قصاص فيه وعليه الأرش في ماله، وإن كان أذن القاطع سكاً أي صغيرة الخلقة وأذن المقطوع صحيحة كبيرة كان بالخيار إن شاء ضمنه نصف الدية، وإن شاء قطعها على صغرها. وكذلك لو كانت أذن القاطع مقطوعة أو خرماء أو مشقوقة كان المقطوع بالخيار وإن كانت الناقصة هي المقطوعة كان له حكومة عدل لا قصاص فيه. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن محمد : ولو قطع المارن وهو أرنبه الأنف ففيها القصاص، وإن قطع من أصله لا قصاص عليه لأنه عظم وليس بمفصل ولا قصاص في العظم. قال أبو حنيفة : لو قطع ذكره من أصله أو . من الحشفة اقتص منه لأنه أمكن استيفاؤه وعلى سبيل المساواة إذ له حد معلوم فأشبه اليد من الكوع . قال رحمه الله :
Chapter of Retribution for Injuries Below the Soul. Qisas is carried out by cutting the hand from the joint, even if the hand of the cutter (the one inflicting qisas) is larger; similarly the foot, the tip of the nose, and the ear. Due to His statement: 'And wounds are qisas' (al-Ma'idah 5:45), i.e., subject to qisas; due to His statement: 'And the tooth for the tooth' (al-Ma'idah 5:45). Qisas is based on equivalence; so wherever equivalence can be observed, qisas is obligatory; where not, then not. And equivalence is possible in these things we mentioned, and no attention is paid to the size of the limb because it does not cause difference in benefit. And if we say that the basis is equality in benefit, then the right hand is not cut for the left, nor a healthy hand for a paralyzed one, nor a woman's hand for a man's, nor a free man's hand for a slave's. And he restricted with the phrase 'from the joint' because if he cuts it from elsewhere than the joint, there is no qisas in it. And in al-Nawadir, al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on both, that if he cuts the fatty part of the ear (shahm al-udhun), qisas is carried out; if he cuts half the ear and it is possible to cut exactly similar, qisas is carried out, because the fatty part of the ear has known limit and the ear has known joints; so if he cuts something from it, it can be known from which joint the cut was made, so qisas is possible. Likewise, if he cuts the cartilage of the ear with a cut in which qisas is possible, qisas is carried out, done with an iron instrument or otherwise. But if he pulled his ear and tore off its fatty part, there is no qisas, and he must pay arsh (compensation) from his property. And if the ear of the cutter is 'sakk' (i.e., small in size) and the ear of the victim is healthy and large, the victim has a choice: if he wishes, he can exact half diyah; if he wishes, he can have it cut on its smallness. Similarly, if the ear of the cutter was already cut or perforated or split, the victim has a choice; and if the deficient one is the victim, then he gets hukumat 'adl (equitable compensation) and no qisas. In Nawadir Ibn Samā'ah from Muhammad: If he cuts the tip of the nose (al-mārn), which is the soft part of the nose, qisas applies; if he cuts from its root, no qisas because it is bone, and there is no joint, and no qisas in bone. Abu Hanifa said: If he cuts his penis from its root or from the glans (hashafah), qisas is carried out because it is possible to exact it in equality, as it has a known limit, so it resembles the hand from the wrist. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said:
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on qisas for injuries less than death. The matn states that qisas is to be performed by cutting the hand from the joint, even if the perpetrator's hand is larger than the victim's; similarly for the foot, the tip of the nose, and the ear. The commentary provides the Quranic basis: 'And wounds are qisas' and 'the tooth for the tooth.' The key principle is that qisas is based on equivalence (mumathala); wherever exact equivalence can be achieved, qisas is wajib; where not, compensation (arsh or hukuma) applies. Equivalence is possible in these specified body parts because they have defined joints and known shapes. Size difference does not matter because it does not affect benefit. However, there are several restrictions: (1) The cut must be at the joint; cutting elsewhere (e.g., through bone) does not permit qisas. (2) The right may not be cut for the left, nor a healthy hand for a paralyzed one, nor a woman's for a man's, nor a free man's for a slave's, because these differ in benefit and value. Then the commentary brings several details from the Nawadir (rare transmissions) on the ear: cutting the fatty part or half the ear can be subject to qisas if precise equivalence is achievable; pulling and tearing (not cutting) precludes qisas. Options for the victim when the perpetrator's ear is smaller or defective. Also from Muhammad: cutting the tip of the nose allows qisas; cutting from the root (bone) does not. Abu Hanifa adds that cutting the penis from the root or glans allows qisas because it has a known limit.

Key Terms
المفصل (al-mifṣal): The joint of a limb, used as the precise point for qisas cutting.
الأرش (al-arsh): Fixed compensation for injury when qisas is not possible.
حكومة عدل (ḥukūmat ʿadl): Equitable compensation determined by a judge when injury has no fixed arsh.
المماثلة (al-mumāthala): Equivalence or exact correspondence, the central principle for qisas.
حد معلوم (ḥad maʿlūm): A known limit or boundary that enables precise retaliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a valid condition for qisas on cutting a hand?
A) The hand must be cut from the joint.
B) The perpetrator's hand must be the same size as the victim's.
C) The right hand must be cut for the right hand.
D) The victim and perpetrator must be of equal status (free for free, slave for slave).

Key Takeaway
Qisas wajib on limbs with joints only when exact equivalence in side, state, and status is possible; otherwise compensation (arsh/hukuma) applies.

Qisas for Eye and Scalp Wound: Condition of Mumathala
Ruling with proof for qisas on eye and scalp wound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qisas for eye injury and scalp wound, and apply the condition of mumathala to determine when qisas is wajib.

Core Idea
Qisas is only wajib when exact equivalence (mumathala) is possible; otherwise, compensation must be taken.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on qisas for injuries to the eye and scalp, based on the principle of mumathala. The text from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the conditions under which retaliation is obligatory, citing the Quran and the practice of 'Ali (ra). Understanding these conditions is critical for correctly applying qisas in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two injury types
The passage discusses two types of injuries: injury to the eye (specifically loss of vision vs. plucking) and scalp wound (shajjah). Note the different rulings for each.
2
Determine the condition for each
For the eye: if vision is lost but the eye remains intact, qisas is wajib; if the eye is plucked out, qisas is not wajib. For the scalp wound: if the wound covers the same area on both parties, qisas is wajib; if the victim's wound is larger, the victim has the option of qisas for the equivalent portion or taking compensation (arsh).
3
Locate the dalil
The Quranic verse 'And the eye for the eye' (5:45) provides the general obligation, but its practical application depends on mumathala. The method for eye injury (heated mirror) is derived from the incident during the caliphate of 'Uthman (ra), where 'Ali (ra) demonstrated the procedure.
The dalil is qati' al-thubut (Quran) but zanni al-dalala in its specific application.
4
Understand the principle of mumathala
Mumathala requires exact equivalence in type, amount, and manner of injury. If exact equivalence is impossible (e.g., plucked eye), qisas is not permitted; instead, financial compensation (diyah/arsh) is taken.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence of the mumathala condition: in cases where equivalence is possible, qisas is carried out; otherwise, the victim receives compensation. This distinguishes the Hanafi approach from schools that may allow qisas more broadly.
The method of using a heated mirror is specific to the Hanafi school, based on the practice of 'Ali (ra).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأذن والعين أن ذهب ضوءها وهي قائمة وإن قلعها لا والسن وإن تفاوتا وكل شجة تتحقق فيها الممائلة) لقوله تعالى والعين بالعين [المائدة: ٤٥] يعني لو ضرب العين فأذهب ضوءها قائمة يجب القصاص لأنه أمكن بأن تحمى لها المرآة وتجعل على وجهه قطن رطب وتشد عينه الأخرى ثم تقرب المرآة من عينه بخلاف ما إذا انقلعت حيث لا يقتص منه لعدم إمكان رعاية المماثلة، وكانت هذه الحادثة وقعت في زمن عثمان رضي الله عنه فشارو الصحابة فقال علي رضي الله تعالى عنه: يجب القصاص فبين إمكان الاستيفاء بالطريق التي ذكرناها. ثم هنا لم يعتبر الكبر والصغر حتى أجرى القصاص في الكل بأستيفاء الكل واعتبر بالشجة في الرأس إذا كانت استوعبت رأس ذلك المشجوج وهي لم تستوعبه رأس الشاج فأثبت للمشجوج الخيار إن شاء اقتص وأخذ بقدر شجته وإن شاء أخذ أرش ذلك لأن ما لحقه من الشين أكثر لأن الشجة المستوعبة لما بين قرينه أكثر شيئاً من الشجة التي لم تستوعب ما بين قرينه بخلاف قطع العضو فإن الشين فيه لا يختلف وكذا منفعته لا تختلف فلم يمكن إلا القصاص لوجود المساواة فيه من كل وجه، وإذا قلعت لا يجب حيث لا يمكن المماثلة إذ لا قدرة لنا أن نفعل به كما فعل من غير زيادة ولا نقصان فلهذا لا يجب القصاص.
And the ear and the eye: that its light goes while it is remaining, (qisas is required), and if it is plucked, no. And the tooth even if they differ, and every scalp wound in which the exact equivalence is possible. Due to His statement, the Exalted: 'And the eye for the eye' [5:45]. Meaning, if he struck the eye and removed its light while it remained, qisas is obligatory because it is possible: a mirror can be heated and placed facing his eye, wet cotton is placed on his face, and his other eye is tied, then the mirror is brought near his eye. Contrary to if it is plucked out, where qisas is not taken from him because exact equivalence cannot be observed. And this incident occurred in the time of 'Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, and he consulted the Companions, and 'Ali, may Allah the Most High be pleased with him, said: 'Qisas is obligatory,' and he clarified the possibility of extraction by the method we mentioned. Then here, bigness and smallness are not considered, so qisas is carried out in the whole by taking the whole. And it was considered in the scalp wound in the head: if it covered the entire head of the wounded person but does not cover the head of the wounder, then the wounded person is given the choice: if he wishes, he may take qisas and take the measure of his wound, and if he wishes, he may take the compensation for that, because the disfigurement that befalls him is more, because the wound that covered the area between his two temples is more substantial than the wound that did not cover the area between his two temples. Unlike cutting a limb, where the disfigurement does not differ, and similarly its benefit does not differ; so nothing is possible except qisas because equality is found in every respect. And if it is plucked out, it is not obligatory because equivalence is not possible, since we have no power to do to him as he did without increase or decrease; therefore qisas is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rulings on qisas for injuries to the eye and scalp wounds, based on the principle of mumathala (exact equivalence). The author first states the rule for the eye: if the eye's vision is destroyed but the eye remains intact, qisas is wajib because exact retaliation is possible by using a heated mirror and protecting the other eye with wet cotton. This is supported by the Quranic verse 'the eye for the eye'. The incident from the time of Caliph 'Uthman, where 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) demonstrated the method, confirms the Hanafi position. However, if the eye is completely plucked out, qisas is not wajib because it is impossible to ensure exact equivalence without addition or deficiency. Then the passage discusses scalp wounds (shajjah): if the wound covers the same area on the victim's head as on the offender's head, qisas is due. If the victim's wound is larger, the victim has the option to take qisas for the exact measure or to take compensation ('arsh) for the excess, because the disfigurement is greater. In contrast, for a limb, the disfigurement and benefit are uniform, so only qisas is possible if equality exists.

Key Terms
الممائلة (al-mumathala): Exact equivalence in type, amount, and manner between the injury inflicted and the retaliation.
الشجة (al-shajjah): A wound on the head or face that reaches the bone or deeper.
القصاص (al-qisas): The prescribed retaliation for intentional injury or killing, carried out by a competent authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When the eye is completely plucked out
B) When the eye's vision is destroyed but the eye remains intact
C) Only if the victim loses both eyes
D) Always, regardless of the condition of the eye

Key Takeaway
Qisas for the eye and scalp wound is only wajib when mumathala (exact equivalence) is achievable; otherwise, compensation is prescribed.

Qisas — Retaliation

Tooth Qisas: Extraction vs Filing – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tooth qisas method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi rivayat on tooth qisas and explain the practical consequence of each.

Core Idea
Tooth qisas has two valid rivayat: extraction or filing—both seek exact equivalence.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rāʼiq presents an internal Hanafi dispute regarding the method of qisas for a completely pulled tooth. The author, Zayn al-Dīn ibn Nujaym, expresses surprise that most commentators contradicted the text of Al-Hidāyah on this point. Understanding this dispute is crucial for a qāḍī who must decide between extracting the offender's tooth or filing it down to the flesh while maintaining the principle of exact equivalence (mumāthalah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
The precise legal question is: When a victim's tooth is completely pulled out, should the offender's corresponding tooth be extracted (qalʿ/nazʿ) as qisas, or should it be filed down (bard) with a file to the level of the flesh?
2
State the two Hanafi rivāyāt
One rivāyah from Al-Qudūrī and Al-Sarakhsī favors filing. The other rivāyah from Shaykh al-Islām and Imam Muḥammad (in Al-Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr) favors extraction. The author notes that the author of Al-Wiqāyah adopted the extraction view in his matn.
The passage also mentions that for partial breakage of a tooth, filing is agreed upon by all.
3
Analyze the underlying principle
Both rivāyāt aim for mumāthalah (exact equivalence). Proponents of extraction argue that qālʿ (plucking) is the direct analogue to the injury. Proponents of filing argue that extraction risks inequality—the offender's tooth might be larger or smaller, or extraction might cause additional damage.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilāf)
In application, the qāḍī must choose one method. If extraction can be performed with confidence in exact equivalence, the first rivāyah applies. If there is risk of excess or deficiency, filing becomes the safer option.
Later Hanafi fatwā often prefers extraction when feasible, or filing as a precaution.
5
Note the author's critique
Ibn Nujaym criticizes most commentators for ignoring the text of Al-Hidāyah entirely—they neither accepted nor rejected it but simply presented the opposite view. He suggests that this shows the dispute was well-known.
This highlights the importance of checking multiple copies and commentary traditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الهداية: ولو قلع السن من أصله يقلع الثاني تماثلاً. قال صاحب الكافي وعامة شراح الكتاب في هذا المقام ولو قلع السن من أصله لا يقلع سنه قصاصاً لتعذر اعتبار المماثلة فربما تفسد المماثلة ولكن تبرد بالمبرد إلى موضع أصل السن، وعزاه الشارح إلى المبسوط. أقول: أسلوب تحريرهم ههنا محل تعجب فإن أحداً منهم لم يتعرض لما ذكر في الكتاب لا بالرد ولا بالقبول بل ذكروا المسئلة على خلاف ما ذكر في الكتاب وكان من دأب الشراح التعرض لما الكتاب إما بالقول وإما بالرد فكأنهم لم يروا أصلاً، نعم القول الذي نقلته ههنا عن المصنف غير مذكور في بعض النسخ لكنه واقع في كثير من النسخ ليس بمثابة أن لا يطلع عليه أحد من الشرائع، كيف وقد أخذه صاحب الوقاية فذكره في متنه حيث قال: ولا وقود في عظم إلا في السن فتقلع إن قلعت وتبرد إن كسرت وكان ما أخذه متن الوقاية هو الهداية كما صرح به صاحبه وكذا ذكره في كثير من المتون ثم إن التحقيق ههنا هو أنه إذا قلع سن غيره هل يقلع سنه قصاصاً أم يبرد بالمبرد إلى أن ينتهي إلى اللحم؟ فيه روايتان كما أفصح عنه في المحيط البرهاني حيث قال: إن كانت الجناية بكسر بعض السن يؤخذ من سن الكاسر بالمبرد مقدار ما كسر من سن الآخر وهذا بالاتفاق، وإن كانت الجناية بقلع سن ذكر القدوري أنه لا يقلع سن القالع ولكن يبرد سن القالع بالمبرد إلى أن ينتهي إلى اللحم ويسقط الباقي، وإليه مال شمس الائمة السرخسي. وذكر شيخ الإسلام في شرحه أنه يقلع سن القالع وإليه أشار محمد في الجامع الصغير حيث ذكر بلفظ النزع والنزع والقلع واحد. وفي الزيادات نص على القلع إلى هنا لفظ المحيط.
And in Al-Hidayah: 'If the tooth is pulled from its root, the second tooth is pulled for equivalence.' The author of Al-Kafi and most commentators of the book in this place (say): 'And if the tooth is pulled from its root, his tooth is not pulled as qisas because exact equivalence is difficult; maybe the equivalence would be spoiled, but it is filed with a file down to the place of the root of the tooth.' And the commentator attributed it to Al-Mabsut. I say: Their style of writing here is a wonder, for none of them addressed what was mentioned in the book, neither by acceptance nor rejection; rather they mentioned the opposite of what was in the book, while it was the practice of commentators to address the book either by approval or rejection, as if they never saw it at all. Yes, the statement I have narrated here from the author is not mentioned in some copies, but it occurs in many copies, not to the extent that none of the commentators saw it. Indeed, the author of Al-Wiqayah took it and mentioned it in his matn, saying: 'There is no qisas in bone except in the tooth; so it is pulled if it was pulled, and filed if it was broken.' And what he took from the matn of Al-Wiqayah is Al-Hidayah, as its author explicitly stated, and similarly he mentioned it in many matns. Then the investigation here is: if he pulled the tooth of another, should his tooth be pulled as qisas, or should it be filed with a file until it reaches the flesh? There are two rivayat, as explained in Al-Muhit al-Burhani, where he said: 'If the injury was by breaking part of a tooth, then from the tooth of the breaker, an amount equal to what was broken of the other's tooth is taken by filing, and this is by agreement. If the injury was by pulling a tooth, Al-Quduri stated that the tooth of the puller is not pulled, but rather the tooth of the puller is filed with a file until it reaches the flesh, and the remainder falls off, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi inclined to this. And Shaykh al-Islam mentioned in his commentary that the tooth of the puller is pulled, and Muhammad indicated as much in Al-Jami' al-Saghir, where he used the word 'naz' (extraction), and naz' and qal' are the same. And in Al-Ziyadat, it is explicitly stated as qal'. Up to here is the wording of Al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding qisas for a tooth that has been completely pulled out. The issue is: should the offender's corresponding tooth be extracted (qal') as retaliation, or should it be filed down (bard) to the point where the flesh begins, to match the loss of the victim? The author notes that most commentators contradict the text of Al-Hidayah on this point, which leads him to express surprise. He traces the dispute to two rivayat: one from Al-Quduri and Al-Sarakhsi who prefer filing, and another from Shaykh al-Islam and Imam Muhammad (in Al-Jami' al-Saghir) who favor extraction. The passage clarifies that for partial breakage of a tooth, there is agreement on filing. The author leaves the matter as an investigation with two positions. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school may vary; later fatwa works often prefer extraction when exact equivalence can be achieved, but the text does not explicitly resolve which is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
البراد/المبرد (al-bard/al-mibrad): Filing with a file; a method of extracting an equal amount of tooth without pulling it entirely.
النزع (al-naz'): Extraction; pulling out the tooth completely.
الرواية (al-riwayah): A transmitted opinion from an early Hanafi authority, here referring to two conflicting transmissions on tooth qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Hanafi authorities are associated with the rivaya favoring extraction?
A) Al-Quduri and Al-Sarakhsi
B) Shaykh al-Islam and Imam Muhammad
C) Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad
D) Ibn Abi Layla and al-Awza'i

Key Takeaway
Tooth qisas in the Hanafi school has two transmitted methods—extraction and filing—allowing flexibility to achieve exact equivalence.
